Odin’s offices felt like a cathedral to power, glass and chrome and quiet money. The elevator doors opened onto a private floor where sound seemed to hush on command. Black-and-white photos lined the corridor: premieres, award nights, a century of cinema codified into a family’s legacy. In every frame, Odin towered at the center, a force wrapped in bespoke suits and cool judgment. Thor and Loki flanked him in different eras, golden and dark, sunlight and shadow.

Rio scanned exit routes, camera placements, the burly receptionist with boxer’s knuckles, the double security door that buzzed open after Maria’s name cleared. Bucky’s posture barely shifted as they walked, loose on the surface, taut beneath. Natasha’s eyes skimmed every reflective surface. Yelena looked bored, which for her meant razor alert. Clint drifted a step behind, too casual to be anything but dangerous. Alice stuck close to Maria, already plotting threads of network access with that fox-bright look she wore when a system dared her to try it.

The inner office was a study in understated restraint: a long walnut table, a wall of glass with the city dropping away beneath, a sideboard with crystal decanters that had probably witnessed more confessions than a church. Odin rose as they entered. He was older than his photographs, deeper lines, a silver beard trimmed like stonework, but he carried himself like gravity wore his name.

“Ms. Vidal,” he said. His voice was a low bell. “Ms. Hill.”

“Mr. Odinson,” Maria replied. “Thank you for making time.”

Thor and Loki rose beside him. Thor’s handshake was an earnest pressure. “We’re very sorry,” he said, voice softer than his frame suggested. “For the young man. We heard.” There was no press-release polish in the way he said it, just grief, plain and human.

Loki inclined his head, green eyes difficult to read. “Horrific,” he murmured. “And unforgivable.”

Odin guided them to the table. Rio didn’t sit until she’d checked every sightline. When she did, it was beside Maria, across from Odin, the table a neutral field that felt anything but.

“We can get to condolences after we get to the point,” Odin said, as if reading Rio’s impatience. “You’re here about Hela.”

Rio didn't preface. “Where is she?”

Odin’s jaw ticked once. “If we knew, you wouldn’t have needed to ask.”

“You’re her father,” Rio said, the question a challenge.

“By blood only,” Odin said. “We severed ties more than ten years ago. She was disowned. No trust, no contact, no access.” His tone left the door open to disbelief but closed it on excuses.

Thor’s hands knotted on the table. “He’s not dissembling.”

Loki rose, crossed to the oak desk, and retrieved a thin, black portfolio. He set it in front of Rio like an offering. “Last known aliases. Rehearsal spaces she favored. The accountants she leaned on. You’ll find breadcrumbs there.”

Bucky leaned forward, elbows on his knees. “Disowned for what?”

Odin’s gaze went to him and back to Rio. “Patterns,” he said. “Violence, manipulation, and an appetite for leverage. She learned to use people long before she had power of her own. When she touched ours… she preferred to shape it through pressure points.”

“People,” Loki supplied, simply.

“People,” Odin agreed.

Yelena clicked her tongue. “Charming.”

Natasha shot her a look that cut the joke off.

Alice’s voice cut in, factual and sharp. “Kyra and Seren were the Cicadas. Renna ran the online mess. Raven watched and followed. Maera handled the muscle. Hela has been the Rat, able to slip into places and break things quietly. We still need to find the Snake, the mastermind.”

Odin’s fingers steepled. “You’re not assuming the Snake is Hela.”

“No,” Rio said flatly. “Hela is a tool. The Snake speaks through tools.”

“Do you have a name?” Loki asked, voice lighter than the room felt.

“Not yet.” Maria’s planner was already open. “We have three paths. Seren and Kyra are in federal custody. Pressure them and see if they talk. Maera’s money trail is exposed; mercs make mistakes. And Hela, rats return to burrows. She will come back to a place she understands: a rigging house, a storage unit, a production office with a back door.”

Odin watched them for a beat. “Hela nests in people before she nests in places.”

The observation landed hard. Rio thought of the note they’d found, of Hela’s history with Agatha. She thought of the way access had always been Hela’s currency.

“Which brings us to the part you won’t like.”

“Say it,” Rio said.

“Hela remained close with Evanora,” Odin said. “Your client’s mother.”

The room stilled. A small, brittle sound escaped Alice, like air leaving a balloon. Yelena swore under her breath. Clint’s eyes went flat. Natasha didn’t move at all.

“Define ‘close,’” Maria said, voice tempered steel.

“Lunches,” Loki answered. “Sporadic calls. An envelope delivered now and then, a ticket, a pass, an invitation. Nothing illegal we could prove. But Evanora likes feeling important. Hela enjoys making people feel important when it suits her.”

Thor’s mouth compressed. “We tried to cut that tie when we cut ours. It clearly didn’t take.”

Heads turned. Evanora, Agatha’s mother, had been a window back home: a complicated, often cruel woman who had trafficked in favors and influence. She’d always been publicly hungry for relevance, and privately sharper. Maria’s voice held the careful tone of someone rehearsed in difficult truths.

“We need to bring Evanora in, very carefully,” Maria continued. “She’s been close with several people Hela used. She’s not the Snake, at least, we don’t have that proof, but she’s a door. She’s been useful to Hela at times, and their relationship is… fraught. Evanora has always hated Agatha, and she's made that known publicly many times. She pushed Agatha into compromises she refused. The motive, envy and control, lines up in a way that’s convenient for the Snake.”

Rio’s jaw tightened. A chill slid under her skin. It made terrible sense. Evanora had her own brand of cruelty: small humiliations dressed as lessons, invitations to bed, and power masked as mentorship. Agatha had described it before, how her mother tried to sell her younger self into favor, how she’d refused and been punished by neglect and public cold. If Hela used Evanora as a conduit, it would explain access to sets, to backstage passes, to the soft places in Agatha’s schedule.

“Is Evanora the Snake?” Yelena asked bluntly.

Maria shook her head slowly. “Not confirmed. But she’s a person of interest. She’s a vector. We loop her in very carefully: subpoenas, monitored contact. If Hela is using her, we’ll see the signal. If she is using Hela, we will see the signs.”

Odin’s voice was flat, but it carried weight. “If your inquiry requires legal cover,” he said, “our counsel will help. We will not leak Billy’s name or anything that weaponizes private grief. You have my word.”

Rio reached for the portfolio. As she did, Thor’s big hand rested briefly on the leather, stopping her, not to challenge, but to add weight.

“Ms. Vidal,” he said. “We cannot pretend this is just a professional matter. Hela… was loved by Agatha once. She will use that. And when she is cornered, she becomes reckless. Protect your client’s heart, too.”

The words were simple, and they landed like a stone thrown into Rio’s chest. She thought of Agatha’s trembling hands. The note Hela had left. Rio saw the order in the phrase Don't let love be the breach.

They traded lists. Alice wanted server access, rehearsal logs, anything Hela might have ghosted through during her stagehand days. Maria would coordinate with authorities to get hard warrants. Odin promised private resources, investigators, contacts in the shipping industry, and historical badge logs from studios.

“There’s another truth,” Loki said, idle-mischief stripped from him. “Hela liked to be seen as the deserving underdog. She hated people who made it without her. Envy is a flavor she knows well.”

Bucky closed his hands into fists, then relaxed them into palms on his knees. “We need to keep it tight. No publicity about Billy. No leaks about the price the Seven put on Agatha. This stays between us and law enforcement. We’re not giving them oxygen.”

Maria nodded. “Agreed. We loop Evanora carefully. If she’s being used, we’ll see patterns: gifts, sudden tickets, voice calls, bank transfers. Alice, I want a forensics run on every contact point between her and Hela’s known aliases.”

Alice’s fingers were already moving. “On it.”

Rio looked at them all, feeling the old soldier’s checklist take over again: priorities, containment, leverage. She also felt something else, deeper and rawer: the knowledge that someone who’d once slept beside Agatha and lied to her face was now stalking the edges of their life. It was a cruelty of an intimate scale.

Thor stood. “One final note. If you need muscle in the field, tell me. I have resources that can clear an alley or hold a doorway.”

Loki rose with a small, sardonic bow. “And I can make a little mischief on your behalf.”

Yelena smirked. “As long as mischief involves knives.”

They left with plans: dossiers, subpoenas, old rehearsal logs to be scanned, storage facilities to be visited. Odin’s team promised discretion; Maria promised motion. Rio gathered the portfolio, feeling its weight like a map to where Hela might be hiding. The conversation had given them paths to walk, some legal, some sharp, some surgical. And a line in the sand: Evanora would be approached, but not trusted.

Outside, as they moved toward the elevator, Maria spoke softly, more to Rio than to anyone else. “We bring her in slowly,” she said. “If Evanora is innocent, she’ll be furious we suspected her. If she’s complicit, she’ll panic. Either way, we’ll see what she reveals.”

Rio nodded. “And Hela? We find her. We finish this.”

The elevator rode down. The city spread below like a board waiting for pieces to move.

They stepped into the corridor. The day outside felt harsher somehow, the light thin. But they had names, leverage, and a plan. They had allies who could open doors and others who could close them. They had a vow: no more surprises.

“Let’s go hunt,” Rio said.

And the team moved, out of the tower, into the city, carrying files like weapons and grief like a vow.

 

The terrace was quiet, the faint hum of the pool’s filter the only sound. The moon threw its silver light across the water, rippling against the tiles. Agatha sat curled in on herself, arms around her knees, sweater pulled tight despite the warm evening. She stared so hard at the pool’s shifting surface that she didn’t register the sliding door open behind her.

Rio stepped out, bare feet silent on the flagstones, and crossed to her. “Hey.”

Agatha’s head turned slightly, eyes dark and tired. “It’s her, isn’t it?” She overheard Maria and Natasha talking earlier about Evanora possibly being the Snake.

Rio sank onto the chaise beside her, careful not to crowd. “We can’t confirm, not officially. But the evidence is…” She exhaled through her nose. “It fits too well. The Snake.”

Agatha let out a laugh, sharp, bitter, humorless. “I should have known. I should have known the second I heard the codename.”

Rio tilted her head. “Why?”

“Because she used it before.” Agatha’s voice broke. “Not to me, never to my face, but on sets, crews would whisper it. They thought it was a joke. Snake, viper, whatever. It wasn’t.”

Rio stayed quiet. She knew when Agatha needed silence more than questions.

Agatha rubbed her arms, nails biting through the sweater. “The first time she hit me, I was five. A commercial audition. I forgot a line. She smiled all the way home. Then she took off her ring and backhanded me with it. Told me if I wanted to be worth anything, I’d bleed for it.” Her laugh was ragged. “So I bled.”

Rio’s jaw locked, every muscle in her body tightening. But she kept her voice calm. “You don’t have to—”

“I want to,” Agatha interrupted, eyes fixed on the pool. “I need you to know.”

Rio nodded once.

“She’d dig her nails into my arms on sets if I flubbed a take. I still have scars. Once, I bombed a chemistry read, and she locked me in a bathroom stall at the studio. Told me not to come out until I’d cried hard enough to look like I cared. I was thirteen.”

Rio’s hand slid over hers, grounding. “Agatha…”

“At fifteen, I was done.” Agatha’s voice sharpened, brittle with old fury. “Lilia had started helping me by then, so I told Evanora I was finished. She screamed until her voice went hoarse, then laughed in my face. Said I’d come crawling back, that without her I was nothing. That I’d never make it unless I did what she told me: smile, sleep with the right people, sell my soul. I left and never looked back.”

For a moment, her mask cracked, tears glinting in her lashes. “But I should have known. Snake. Viper. It was all right there. I should have known she’d never stop trying to destroy me.”

Rio finally moved, shifting closer until she could frame Agatha’s face in her hands. Her thumbs brushed away tears that hadn’t yet fallen. “Listen to me. You cut her out when you were barely more than a child. You survived her. And now you’ve built a life she’ll never touch, no matter how hard she tries. That isn’t her victory, Agatha, it’s yours.”

Agatha met her gaze, something fierce and broken all at once in her eyes. “She was supposed to love me, Rio. Not—” Her voice cracked. “Not this. Not… this.”

Rio pulled her into her chest, arms wrapping tight, protective. “She didn’t know how. But that’s not on you. That’s on her.”

Agatha clung to her, pressing her face into Rio’s shoulder like she could hide there. For a long time, neither spoke. Just the sound of their breathing and the quiet lap of water against tile.

Finally Agatha whispered, muffled, “I hate that I still care. That some part of me still wants her to say she’s proud of me.”

Rio kissed her temple. “That’s the little girl in you talking. The one she hurt. You don’t owe her your care, mi amor. You don’t owe her anything.”

Agatha pulled back enough to look at her, eyes wet but blazing. “Then help me end this. If she really is the Snake, if Evanora is behind all of this, I don’t want mercy. I don’t want understanding. I want her stopped. For good.”

Rio searched her face, seeing the rawness there, the steel underneath. She nodded slowly. “Then that’s what we’ll do.”

Agatha exhaled, a shudder leaving her body. She pressed her forehead to Rio’s again, drawing strength from the closeness. “You’ll stay with me, right? No matter what comes next?”

“Always,” Rio promised, and for once, it didn’t feel like a vow weighed down by guilt or failure. It felt like the truth.

They stayed like that for a long while, two women bound by pasts that refused to stay buried, and by a present they refused to give up. The stars wheeled overhead, silent witnesses.

When Agatha finally pulled back, her voice was steadier. “I cut ties with her at fifteen. I thought that was the end. But if she wants a war, Rio, she picked the wrong daughter to underestimate.”

Rio’s lips curved, fierce and proud. “That’s the Agatha I know.”

Agatha let herself smile, tired, aching, but real. And for the first time in days, the fire in her chest burned brighter than the fear.

Chapter End Notes

Evanora can kiss my white, pasty ass. I hate her more than words can say. Agatha deserves better.

A quick rundown of the Seven
Raven Corvin-The Crow, was Agatha's old assistant, fired for theft of underwear and jewelry (In Custody)
Renna-The Fox, ran the top fan Accounts for Agatha. Agatha rejected her advances due to her being underage (Deceased)
Kyra and Seren Auden- The Cicadas, Very Early stans of Agatha, ran old fan projects, funded one of Agatha's indie films, got her name trending. Agatha gave them a shout-out one time, but never again (In Custody)
Maera Rourke- The Coyote, was Agatha's old bodyguard, now a gun for hire (Still at Large)
Hela Odinson- The Rat, Agatha's first love, feels envious of Agatha for being what she never could hope to be (Still at Large)
Evanora Harkness- The Snake, is Agatha's Mom, hates that Agatha is succeeding without her, and cast her aside. Wants to own Agatha (Still at Large)

Funeral Pyre

Chapter Summary

“What we have once enjoyed deeply we can never lose. All that we love deeply becomes a part of us.”- Helen Keller

Chapter Notes

Two chapters in one day!! Look at me go.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

News of the attack on Agatha’s home, and Billy’s death, had yet to break in the days that followed. Jen, Maria, and the Odinsons were working overtime to keep it quiet, pulling every string they had to delay the inevitable storm. But silence only stoked curiosity. The media and fans were already speculating, noting the heavy increase in security, the rare absence of Agatha’s public presence, and the sudden hush from people usually so active. The world could feel something was wrong.

Inside the house, grief hung thick in the air. Wanda sat curled on the sofa in the living room, her hand entwined with Vision’s, her face hollowed by days of crying and sleepless nights. Vision, usually a man of calm composure, looked frayed, his arm tight around her as though holding her together with sheer will. On the other side of the couch sat Tommy, staring blankly at the wall. His eyes were red, but he wasn’t crying anymore. It was as if something inside him had shut down, leaving only a shell behind.

No one spoke for a long while. The silence was oppressive, punctuated only by the occasional creak of the old house settling. Finally, Wanda’s voice cut through the stillness, low but steady, fueled by a mix of sorrow and fire.

“We’ve decided to put out a statement,” she said, her tone leaving no room for argument. “Billy’s funeral is tomorrow. The public needs to know. I want them to know that my baby boy was murdered. I don’t care about speculation, I don’t care about backlash, I want it known. What I will not allow is for his life, or his death, to be reduced to whispers and rumor.”

Her eyes flashed dangerously as she looked around the room, daring anyone to contradict her. No one did. Even Agatha, who sat with her knees pulled to her chest near the fireplace, only lowered her gaze in sorrowful agreement.

Rio was the one to answer, her voice calm but resolute. She sat forward in her chair, elbows braced on her knees, the weight of command heavy in her posture. “Hela already knows we’ve unmasked her,” Rio said evenly. “She’ll likely move to release her own narrative soon, the same way we put names to the others. That choice is yours, Wanda. Whatever you decide, my team will adjust and protect around it. The only thing we ask, what has to remain hidden, is the identity of the Snake. Evanora cannot know she’s been identified. If she spooks and disappears into the shadows, we may never get another chance to bring her down.”

The words hung in the air like a warning bell. Wanda’s fingers tightened around Vision’s hand, and Vision pressed a kiss to her temple, murmuring something too quiet for anyone else to hear. Tommy’s jaw clenched, his first flicker of reaction in hours, and for a moment, the resemblance to Billy was so stark it broke Agatha’s heart all over again.

No one argued with Wanda’s grief, but everyone understood Rio’s warning: in a war fought from the shadows, one misstep could cost them everything.

“It will be smart for Rio and Agatha to also put out statements of their own,” Jen said, her voice hoarse from days of crying. Agatha and Rio nodded. 

Wanda’s Statement

My son, Billy Maximoff, was murdered.

I will not soften it. I will not dress it up for comfort. He was taken from me—taken from us—by a group of cowards hiding behind masks, shadows, and lies. They want silence. They want fear. They will get neither from me.

Billy was light. He was laughter that filled a room, kindness that made people better just by knowing him. He was not only my son, but my best friend, and a brother to many who loved him. The world is colder without him, and no words will ever fill the space he leaves behind.

To those responsible: you did not break me. You did not break my family. You will pay for what you’ve done, whether in courts or in fire, but you will not win. Every second I breathe will be spent honoring Billy’s life and making sure his name means more than the cruelty that ended it.

To those who mourn with us: we ask for privacy to grieve, but know this, your love is felt. Your prayers are heard. Billy mattered, and together we will make sure he is remembered not as a victim, but as a son, a brother, and a soul too bright to dim.

My baby deserved more years. He deserved the world. And because he cannot have them, I will fight to protect everyone else’s children from suffering the same.

—Wanda Maximoff

 

Rio’s Statement

On behalf of S.H.I.E.L.D. Security, I can confirm that a few nights ago, our client’s residence was assaulted by a coordinated strike. One hundred and eight armed intruders were neutralized on the property. Several more were taken into custody and are now in the hands of authorities.

Despite our best efforts, we were not able to prevent the loss of one life: Billy Maximoff. He was more than family to our client. He was part of our own. His death is a tragedy, and as the team leader on site, I carry the weight of not being able to protect him. That weight will never leave me.

Let it be known, every single individual responsible for this atrocity, whether they pulled the trigger or hid behind a keyboard, will be found. They will be named. And they will be brought to justice.

S.H.I.E.L.D. has faced threats on every level, across every border, and we have never failed to adapt, endure, and prevail. We will not start now.

Billy’s life deserved better, and his loss steels our resolve to ensure no other family endures what Wanda’s has. For his memory, and for every innocent threatened, we will not stop.

—Rio Vidal
 Co-CEO, S.H.I.E.L.D. Security

 

Agatha’s Statement 

Billy Kaplan was more than an assistant. He was more than family. He was my heart.

I was there the day he was born. I watched him grow into the brilliant, stubborn, wonderful young man the world came to know. To me, he was a son. To lose him in such a senseless, cruel act shatters me in ways I do not yet know how to put into words.

The group calling themselves the “Seventh Veil” believes they can terrorize me by targeting those I love. They are wrong. What they have done has only deepened my resolve. Billy deserved the future they stole from him. And I will not rest until justice is served—not only for me, but for Wanda, for Tommy, for everyone who loved Billy.

Grief is heavy. It is unrelenting. But I want my fans, my Coven, to know this: Billy was light, and I will carry that light forward. I will honor him with the truth. I will honor him with the fight. And I will not let his story end with violence.

Billy, I love you. Always.

—Agatha

 

Media and fan reactions began pouring in instantly, like a tidal wave. Hashtags like #JusticeForBilly #StandwithWanda #BillysLight #RioProtects are trending worldwide on every major platform.

 

Variety Headline:
 Tragedy Strikes: Agatha Harkness’ Assistant Billy Maximoff Confirmed Dead in Targeted Attack on Actress’ Home

Hollywood reels after confirmation that Billy Maximoff, long-time friend and assistant to actress Agatha Harkness, was killed during a violent assault on her Los Angeles residence. Security sources confirm over 100 armed intruders were neutralized, marking one of the most brazen celebrity-targeted attacks in recent memory. Questions are now being raised about the group calling themselves The Seventh Veil, already linked to stalking and harassment campaigns against Harkness.

 

The Hollywood Reporter:
 “We will not be silenced”: Harkness, Vidal, and Maximoff Release Powerful Statements After Fatal Attack

In statements released early this morning, Wanda Maximoff, Rio Vidal of S.H.I.E.L.D. Security, and Harkness herself called for justice while honoring Billy’s life. Fans and colleagues alike are rallying behind the actress, with hashtags like #JusticeForBilly, #StandWithAgatha, and #StandwithWanda trending globally.

 

New York Times International:
 Paramilitary Tactics in Celebrity Harassment: The Escalation Against Actress Agatha Harkness

The attack on Harkness’ home raises new questions about the lengths extremist fandom-turned-criminal groups will go to exert power and intimidation. Officials confirm investigations are underway into international funding streams tied to The Seventh Veil. Law enforcement agencies warn that this is no longer “celebrity gossip gone wrong,” but organized crime.

@ScarletCoven13: I can’t even breathe right now. Billy wasn’t just Agatha’s assistant, he was FAMILY. He grew up with her, he was always there. The Seven crossed a line they can’t come back from. #JusticeForBilly

@BelowDeckObsessed: I can’t stop crying. This wasn’t some random person, this was WANDA’S SON. How do you hurt her like that? How do you hurt AGATHA like that?? Monsters. Absolute monsters.

@WatcherNoMore: Let’s be real: the headlines should read “Agatha’s team destroys a small army.” I don’t know how they pulled it off but Rio Vidal is basically John Wick at this point. #RioProtects

@ScarletSon: I keep seeing the number “108” and it doesn’t feel real. Imagine that many armed killers showing up at your house, and you survive because your team is THAT good. And then you still lose someone you love. I’m shaking.

@FanOfTheCoven: Billy was the one who always waved at us at premieres, always handed out water to fans waiting. He was kind. He was one of the good ones. My heart is shattered. 💔 #StandWithAgatha

 Caption: He looked at her like a son looks at his mom. This hurts so much. 💔 #RIPBilly

@Paul-Rudd: No parent should have to bury their child. No woman should have to fear armed men storming her home. Sending every ounce of love to Wanda, Agatha, and their family. This has to end.

@Marvel Studios: We are devastated by the tragic loss of Billy Maximoff, who was deeply loved by those on set and off. Our hearts are with Wanda, Agatha, and their families during this painful time. We stand against all forms of violence and harassment.

Agatha, curled into Rio’s side on the couch, sat up a little straighter. “What are they saying?”

Jen scrolled, scanning her feed. “Here’s The Times: ‘Never in Hollywood history has a single residence been targeted with this scale of force, and survived. Agatha Harkness’ security team repelled what sources describe as over one hundred trained mercenaries, making this one of the largest single-site counter-assaults on U.S. soil in recent memory.’”

Natasha, standing by the fireplace with arms crossed, let out a sharp exhale. “They’re not wrong.”

Jen flicked to another article. “The Guardian is calling it ‘a near-mythic defense led by Rio Vidal, whose reputation as a private security executive now borders on legendary.’ They’re comparing it to war reports. Not film news. War reports.”

Rio’s jaw tightened. “They’re not wrong to be stunned. But we don’t want this turning into some gladiator headline. We lost Billy. That’s the only thing people should be focused on.”

Jen softened, lowering the phone. “I agree. But the press is painting this as something no one’s ever seen before. The idea that a celebrity’s home was attacked like a military target? And survived? Journalists don’t even have a frame of reference. They’re calling it ‘unprecedented,’ ‘historic,’ ‘borderline cinematic.’ And all eyes are on us now.”

The room fell silent, the weight of her words pressing down like an unwelcome guest.

The next morning came heavy, gray skies pressing low over the house like the world itself knew what day it was. The air was thick with quiet, the kind of silence that settled into the bones. No one needed to be told what today was.

Upstairs, Agatha sat at the edge of the bed, staring at the black dress laid out across the blankets. She hadn’t worn anything so stark in years. Her hands trembled faintly, not from fear, but from the weight of grief pressing down on her chest. She felt the mattress dip as Rio came out of the bathroom, hair slicked back, already dressed in her suit. No tactical vest, no weapons visible, just black fabric stretched over her tall frame, neat and understated. Still, she looked like steel forged into human form.

Rio knelt in front of her, wordless for a moment, before reaching for Agatha’s hands. “Mi vida,” she murmured, her voice low, steady, grounding. “We’ll get through today.”

Agatha blinked, the sting of tears threatening, but she held them back. Instead, she cupped Rio’s jaw, thumb brushing over her cheekbone. “I don’t know how Wanda is still breathing,” she whispered. “If I lost—” She stopped, unable to finish.

Rio pressed her forehead to Agatha’s. “Don’t,” she said softly. “We can’t go there. Not today. Today we stand with Wanda. With Billy.”

It was enough. Agatha leaned into her, pressing a lingering kiss to her lips before finally rising. Rio helped her into the dress, smoothing the fabric over her shoulders with a tenderness that made Agatha’s chest ache. By the time she slid on her coat and heels, she felt steadier, not whole, but anchored.

Downstairs, the rest of the team moved through the motions with a kind of reverent silence. Natasha adjusted her cuffs with sharp, precise movements. Bucky stood near the window, eyes distant, his suit fitting him like a uniform. Clint fussed with a tie he clearly hated. Alice sat at the kitchen counter, glasses perched on her nose, hair pinned back, for once not glued to her laptop. Even Yelena, usually all sharp humor and snark, was quiet as she laced her boots, jaw set.

Jen and Lilia were waiting near the door, both dressed in black. Lilia clutched her bag like it was armor, her expression unreadable but her eyes rimmed red. Jen looked exhausted, but resolute, a clipboard in hand as if paperwork might keep her from breaking apart.

When Agatha and Rio entered, the group looked up. No one said anything, but the silence shifted, softened. Agatha offered a small, trembling smile. “Let’s go.”

Outside, the air was brisk, biting. Two black SUVs waited in the driveway, engines rumbling low. Sonya and Steve stood at the gates, rifles slung but dressed respectfully, nodding solemnly as the team approached. Wanda’s security will join them today, stationed discreetly along the road with local cops marking the perimeter of the Church. It wasn’t a military operation today, though it felt like one. It was a funeral.

Rio opened the door for Agatha, steadying her as she climbed in. Their fingers lingered together until the last possible second, a lifeline in the storm. Wanda and Vision would meet them at the church, along with Tommy and the few friends they’d chosen to let inside their grief.

As the SUVs pulled out of the drive, the house shrank behind them, its walls still scarred from the siege that had stolen so much. Agatha leaned into Rio’s side, her voice barely above a whisper. “We’ll honor him today. That’s all that matters.”

Rio wrapped an arm around her, pulling her close, her jaw set, her eyes locked on the road ahead. “We’ll honor him,” she said. “And we’ll make sure he’s never forgotten.”

No one else spoke. The engines hummed, tires rolling over wet pavement, carrying them toward a day none of them wanted but all of them would endure, together.

The chapel was small and white-washed, tucked back from the street behind a fringe of oaks that rattled softly in the wind. Someone had draped black ribbon along the railings and set lanterns at the steps; their glass panes held the gray morning like little squares of sky. Inside, the air smelled faintly of old wood and lilies. Candles burned along the side aisles in neat rows, tiny constellations trembling in the draft each time the door opened.

They kept the front pew for the family. Wanda and Vision sat in the center, fingers locked so tightly their knuckles were white. Tommy was on Wanda’s other side, shoulders hunched, the brim of his black cap pulled low like a child trying to disappear. Agatha slid in behind them with Rio, one hand resting lightly between Wanda’s shoulder blades in a silent, steadying circle. The team took the pew behind. Natasha, Bucky, Clint, Alice, Yelena, dark suits and solemn faces, eyes moving with the trained awareness that never quite turned off. A few rows farther back, Jen folded a program into a soft fan and didn’t seem to notice she was doing it. Lilia sat beside her, hands clasped in her lap around a handkerchief she hadn’t yet used.

The church filled quietly: producers, grips, hair and makeup artists, studio publicists, neighbors, a couple of old school friends of Billy’s that Vision had tracked down. No cameras inside, Wanda’s single hard condition, and the ushers were gentle but firm about phones. Outside on the sidewalk, a cluster of fans kept vigil behind sawhorse barricades, holding flowers and homemade signs: We love you, Billy. Thank you for being kind. For Wanda, for Agatha, for family. A few of them cried openly. Sonya and Steve coordinated with local police to keep the street calm.

When the organ began, it did so softly, as if it, too, was careful not to jar the room. A simple melody, the kind you could hum without realizing it, rose to the rafters and diffused through the candlelight. The officiant wasn’t a priest; she introduced herself simply as Elise, someone who did a lot of “life ceremonies” for people who didn’t fit neatly into boxes. She welcomed everyone, acknowledged the unimaginable, and made space for whatever each person believed. “Grief,” she said, “is the price love is willing to pay. But love is patient. It will stay with you even when the grief feels like a storm that won’t end.”

A slideshow began on a canvas screen—no dramatic sound, just the whisper of the projector. Billy at five, missing teeth, holding a sparkler. Billy at twelve with a skateboard, scraped knees and a crooked grin. Billy at nineteen at a wrap party, confetti stuck in his hair. Billy handing Agatha a bottle of water at a scorching premiere, fussing over her collar. Billy singing badly into a hairbrush while Jen filmed and laughed so hard the camera shook. Each picture bloomed and faded. Sometimes the chapel laughed, softly, like a ripple over stones. Sometimes it hushed all at once, particularly when an image of Billy with Wanda and Tommy appeared—mother, sons, cheeks pressed together, the easy warmth of family filling the frame.

Wanda stood when it was her turn to speak. Rio felt Agatha’s breath catch beside her. The podium looked too big under Wanda’s hands, and for a moment she simply rested her fingers on either side of it, gathering breath. The room went very still.

“My son,” she said, and the words were both introduction and invocation. Her voice was hoarse but steady, the kind people get when they’ve sobbed as much as a body can and somehow found another layer of strength. “Billy… was the person you called when you locked yourself out, when you forgot a birthday, when you needed someone to tell you the truth even if you didn’t want to hear it. He was gentle, but not fragile. He was funny, but never unkind. He loved music. He loved cooking disasters, he once tried to flambé pancakes, and we lost a perfectly good curtain. He loved his brother with a fierceness that made me feel like I’d done at least one thing right.”

A soft, watery laugh traveled the pews. Wanda’s mouth tilted; she kept going.

“On sets, he collected strays. You know who you are.” A few people smiled through tears. “He took care of me, even when I was supposed to be taking care of him. That’s who he was. Light in the hallway. Arms when you thought you were falling. He wasn’t famous, but fame never had anything to teach him. He already knew the important things.”

She paused, blinked hard, swallowed.

“I am grateful,” she said, and the word seemed impossible in her mouth and yet here it was. “For twenty-two years. For every version of him I got to know, every joke he told that didn’t land, every hug that did. I am angry. I am broken. I am also not alone. He made sure of that, he built us into each other so that when one of us falls, the others remember how to help them stand. Thank you for loving my boy. Please keep saying his name.” She looked out over the chapel; the force of will in her gaze was almost a tangible thing. “He is not a headline. He is not content. He is our Billy.”

She stepped down, and Vision rose, catching her hand. They kissed each other’s knuckles like it was a vow they’d made long ago and needed to remember how to say it again. Tommy stood briefly, murmured, “He stole my hoodies,” and then sat fast with his face in his hands. The room didn’t laugh—didn’t dare—but it softened, the way a hand softens when it holds something delicate.

Agatha didn’t realize she’d stood until she was already halfway down the aisle. Rio glanced up once, and the look they shared—I’m here—was enough to set Agatha’s spine. At the podium, she laid her palms flat and exhaled through her nose, as if steadying a line on a stage she’d delivered a hundred times and then rewritten the night before.

“I remember when Billy was twelve,” she began, looking out at Wanda, Tommy, Vision. “I was visiting Wanda on set and he was so bored he could have faded through the floor. He asked me if acting was just pretending to pretend. I told him that was what most bad acting was, yes.” A ripple of laughter. “He didn’t blink. He said, ‘Then you’re good at the real kind.’ He was right.” She lifted a shoulder. “He usually was.”

Her smile flickered. “He became my assistant because he was already my shadow. He knew when I needed to take a break before I did. He knew where my spare charger lived. He knew how to make me eat when I was all nerves and no appetite. He knew how to tell me no. Not many people can do that and survive.” A breath, a crowd-smile. It faded. “He was my family. Not because of blood, but because of choice, and constancy, and care. I was there when he was born. He’ll be with me when I die.”

She looked down for the first time, then back up, eyes bright. “I don’t have a lesson. I don’t have a neat bow. I have love, which is messy and greedy and refuses to end when the person you love does. I have gratitude for every minute I got with him. And I have a promise.” She turned slightly toward Wanda. “We will carry him. All of us. We’ll say his name. We’ll keep his joy moving through rooms like a breeze you can’t see but feel anyway.” She swallowed. “I love you, Billy. Thank you for choosing me.”

When she returned to the pew, Rio’s hand found hers and didn’t let go. For a moment, Agatha leaned into her shoulder, a quiet collapse she didn’t allow herself often. Rio angled closer, shielding her from the sightlines of the room without making a scene of it.

The officiant invited anyone else who wished to share. A lighting tech told a story about Billy learning every crew member’s name on day one. A hair stylist confessed he once sang karaoke at wrap because Billy bribed him with cupcakes. Jen spoke about how he’d quietly mailed flowers to a fan who’d lost her dad and never told anyone about it. The chapel held their laughter and their tears like they were both sacred.

Music again. Not a hymn, an acoustic guitar and a voice from the back that sounded like smoke at the edge of a fire, singing about carrying light across dark water. It didn’t overreach. It didn’t try to fix anything. It simply moved through the space and gave people a place to put what they were holding.

Rio didn’t intend to speak. She wasn’t in the program. But when Elise asked whether anyone who had protected Billy wanted to say a word, Agatha’s hand squeezed, and Rio felt something unlock in her chest.

She stood because soldiers stand, even when their legs aren’t sure. The aisle seemed long; she wasn’t built for altars and microphones. She took the podium the way she took a perimeter, quietly, respectfully, eyes scanning before she set them down on the family.

“I don’t… do this,” she said, almost apologetic. “But I owe him a few words.”

Her voice was low, steady, the kind that made people lean in.

“I protect people for a living. Sometimes, I fail.” The words twitched; she stilled them. “That failure is a burden I carry and will keep carrying. But I want to say this: when the worst thing happened, Billy was brave. He did not run from other people’s fear. He stepped toward it. He looked for who needed him. That’s not weakness. That’s the bravest thing I know.”

She drew a breath, let it out through her nose. Her eyes flicked to Wanda and Vision. “You raised a good man. The kind this world can’t afford to lose and can’t stop remembering.” She hesitated, then allowed herself the smallest softening. “I will spend the rest of my life making sure the people who did this never do it again.” She dipped her head, simple and sincere, and stepped down.

On her way back, she passed Natasha in the aisle. Nat didn’t speak; she just caught Rio’s wrist for a heartbeat, a wordless you did right.

The service closed with something Elise called a “litany of names.” She read a line about light, and the congregation answered with “We remember,” each time Billy’s name was spoken. People rose, one by one, to light candles at the side rails. Wanda went first, Vision with her. Tommy followed, lips pressed tight, eyes wet but not spilling. Agatha lit two, one for Billy, one for the version of herself that had known him since he was born, and would have to learn how to walk without him. Rio lit one and didn’t say what it was for. She didn’t have to.

Outside, the sky had cleared to a pale, soft blue. The fans behind the barricades were quiet, as if the tone of the day had seeped into the street. When Wanda stepped out, a hush fell that felt like respect, not voyeurism. Someone began to hum the tune from inside. Others joined; it drifted across the asphalt like a prayer no one had to agree on to share.

At the graveside, small cemetery, low stone wall, sunlight splintering through leaves, the circle drew closer. There was no long sermon. Elise read a few lines Wanda had chosen: about love being the thing that death can’t steal; about memory being a kind of second life. People placed flowers on the lid, simple stems, not arrangements. Agatha’s was a purple iris. Rio’s was a white lily she hadn’t planned to bring; one of the ushers had pressed it into her hand at the chapel door, as if he’d seen something on her face and knew she needed something to set down.

When the first shovelful of earth landed, the sound was softer than Agatha expected. She flinched anyway. Rio’s palm settled at the small of her back. I know. It said enough.

The reception afterward was in the church hall—folding tables draped in black cloth, coffee urns, sandwiches no one was hungry for and ate anyway because the body needs something to do when the heart is trying to remember how. People told stories in clusters. There was laughter, real and high, the kind that bursts out when grief has wrung a person dry and the smallest shard of joy finds a crack. Wanda didn’t leave the corner where she and Vision sat; people came to her in a gentle queue, each one taking her hand and pressing it, speaking briefly, moving on to make room. Tommy hovered near the cookies and didn’t touch any.

Rio stayed close to Agatha without hovering, moving with that quiet awareness that made people unconsciously part around her. She didn’t need to check exits; Natasha and Clint were already placed. Yelena made a show of eating three cookies and glaring at anyone who looked like they might tell her to stop. Alice positioned herself near the door with two uniformed officers, speaking quietly, trading names. Every so often, Sonya’s voice crackled in Rio’s earpiece from the outer perimeter: Street’s calm. Crowd respectful.

Jen eventually perched on the edge of their table with her phone face down. “I’m not taking calls today,” she announced, voice wrecked. “Anyone who needs a quote can read the statements and let that be enough.” No one argued.

Lilia arrived with a small frame under her arm—a candid photo of Billy and Agatha from years ago, heads thrown back, laughing at nothing. She set it beside the coffee urn like an altar and, for the first time all day, smiled.

The formal goodbyes stretched into the slow dissolving that always happens when people don’t want to be the first to leave because leaving means the day is over and the person is still gone. At last, Wanda rose. Rio saw the way Agatha straightened instinctively. The two women met in the center of the room and folded into each other, no words, foreheads pressed together as if to share breath. Rio looked away, not because she was shut out but because some things are for the people inside them alone.

When it was time to go, the team formed around Wanda’s family and Agatha without being asked. Outside, the air had warmed. The fans were gone; the police had cleared the street to give the families privacy. The SUVs lined the curb like punctuation marks ending a sentence no one wanted to write.

At the curb, Wanda hugged Agatha once more and then turned to Rio. For a suspended second, grief and gratitude braided together in her eyes. She reached out, squeezed Rio’s hand hard enough for bone to remember it, and nodded. No promises either of them could make would bring Billy back. But some promises could still be kept.

In the car, Agatha’s head found Rio’s shoulder almost immediately. The hum of the engine, the rhythm of the tires, the low murmur of Clint and Alice in the lead vehicle over comms, all of it layered into a kind of lullaby for people who had slept little and cried too much.

“You were brave,” Rio murmured, not looking away from the window. “He would have been proud of you.”

Agatha’s answer was a breath that trembled and then settled. “He hated when I cried,” she said, a ghost of a smile finding her mouth. “Said it made my eyeliner betray me.”

Rio huffed the smallest laugh. “Then we’ll tell him we kept it together for the makeup department.”

Outside, the city moved the way cities always do, indifferent and alive, horns and crosswalk chimes and a woman in a red coat carrying sunflowers. Inside the SUV, grief rode quietly, not like an enemy anymore but like a companion everyone knew by name. The house waited ahead, with its patched fences and its new cameras and its people who had chosen each other. The work of justice would resume. The hunt would resume. But for the long ride home, they let the silence hold them, and in it, the name they had promised to keep saying threaded through each breath.

Billy.

Chapter End Notes
