The Soldier's Sacrifice

Chapter Summary

Chaos erupts on the way back from the Funeral

Chapter Notes

Please don't hate me!!!

See the end of the chapter for more notes

The motorcade moved like a river of black glass, two SUVs nose to tail along the post-funeral traffic. Inside the lead car, Agatha rested against Rio’s shoulder, the world outside a gray smear of trees and storefronts. Natasha drove, jaw set, eyes flicking between mirrors. Clint rode shotgun, unusually quiet, one hand hovering near the center console where his sidearm was hidden.

Rio’s gaze never stayed still. Every third car behind them. Every pedestrian who paused. The way a motorcycle held their blind spot just a beat too long. Her ribs felt tight, not grief this time, but the old, prickling instinct she’d learned to obey without question.

She felt it before she saw it, the faintest ripple of wrong. She snapped her head left. A black SUV roared out from a side street, too fast, too close. Another surged up behind them, engine screaming.

“Hold on!” Rio barked.

She moved before the words finished. She seized Agatha by the waist, yanked her across the bench, rolled with her, and slammed her own body over the actress just as the world detonated.

Impact slammed into them from both sides, a brutal crunch of steel and glass. The left flank collapsed inward; Natasha’s airbag exploded, pinning her. Clint’s forehead cracked the dash, he slumped, blood streaking his temple. The rear hit whipped them sideways; tires screamed; the SUV fishtailed, clipped a lamppost, and shuddered to a smoking halt across two lanes.

The second SUV—Alice, Yelena, Bucky, took a hard T-bone at the intersection behind them. It spun, skidded backward into a parked van, and wedged at a jagged angle. For a heartbeat, there was only ringing silence and drifting dust.

Then doors slammed. Boots hit asphalt. Figures poured out of the ramming vehicles like a black tide, ten, twelve, more, faces masked, weapons up: pistols, short batons, knives. Last to emerge, unhurried, was Maera Rourke. She was all hard lines and calm breath, hair twisted into a knot that looked like it could cut glass.

Rio shoved off Agatha, ignoring the bolt of pain that ripped through her side. “Stay down,” she rasped. “Do not move.”

Agatha’s fingers caught her wrist. “Rio—”

“Eyes on me.” Rio’s stare pinned her. “Breathe. I come back to you.”

She kicked the crumpled door twice; on the third hit it buckled, then gave. She slid out into the open, boots crunching glass, gun already in her hand. The world narrowed to angles and threats.

“Vidal,” Maera called over the din, a cruel half-smile breaking her severe face. “Finally.”

“You picked the wrong wake to crash,” Rio said, voice like ice.

Maera rolled a shoulder, drew her pistol with lazy confidence. “Said every dead woman ever.”

The first merc rushed her, knife low, pistol high. Rio beat the gun with her forearm, shoved it off-line as the round cracked past her ear, then stepped into the knife hand and snapped the wrist so fast the blade popped free. She palmed the knife, buried it under the attacker’s ribs, and used his body as cover against the second shooter’s muzzle flash. Blood misted her cheek. She shoved the dying man into his partner, kicked the partner’s knee sideways until the joint popped, then shot him once in the sternum without looking.

Three more fanned to encircle. She moved, never in a straight line, half-circles, a slip to step inside a punch, the baton strike she wrenched off a belt mid-motion. A pistol barked; heat lanced across her upper arm. She turned into the pain and drove the stolen baton into the gunman’s throat. He folded, gagging.

Two came together, blade and club. Rio let the knife pass close, feeling it tug fabric at her ribs, then trapped the blade arm, rolled her shoulder, and slung the attacker over her hip onto the cracked yellow line. The club came down; she interposed the fallen man’s forearm; the club hit bone with a sick snap. She snatched the club on the rebound and hammered it into the wielder’s solar plexus, then his ear. He collapsed, twitching.

“Rio!” Agatha’s voice, high, frayed, yanked at the corner of Rio’s focus.

“Stay there, mi vida!” Rio shouted back, never taking her eyes off the threats.

More mercs advanced, cautious now. Civilians had frozen on the sidewalks, phones up, the gasp of a crowd like a held breath. Sirens far away, threading closer. Maera waited beyond the ring, patient, predatory.

“Where’s that other car?” Rio murmured into the mic clipped under her collarbone.

Static. Then Alice, strained: “Pinned—driver side jammed—Yelena’s leg is—working it—two hostiles—”

“Hold,” Rio snapped. “Keep your heads down.”

She reloaded by feel, eyes up. A woman sprinted left, firing wild to force her into cover. Rio didn’t give ground. She cut the angle instead, met the runner at the hood of the wrecked SUV, and slammed a forearm across her jaw, bouncing her skull off the metal until her eyes rolled. Another attacker reached for a grappling line on his harness; Rio fired once; the hook clattered from nerveless fingers; he dropped to his knees with a red mouth opening in his chest.

She caught motion in the reflection of the SUV’s mangled chrome, someone coming fast behind her with a garrote. She ducked under the wire at the last instant, spun inside the loop, and rammed her fist into the man’s kidney twice, then once, hard, to the throat. He reeled, gurgling. She snatched the length of wire and, with one brutal twist, wove it between his forearms and the guard rail post, cinching it and jerking until he was stuck and useless.

Her breath burned. Her side screamed each time she pivoted. Blood slicked her fingers where a round had creased her triceps; warmth spread across her ribs where something was not right. She didn’t slow.

At the second SUV, the passenger door blew outward with a grunt. Yelena, grim, bleeding from the scalp, burst free by sheer rage, dragging a stunned merc by his collar. She threw him headfirst into the curb, stole his knife in one smooth pull, and carved another attacker across the thigh before he understood she was loose. Bucky kicked his door twice, then a third time; it gave with a shriek. He rolled out, came up firing a controlled two-shot pattern that put two mercs on their backs. Alice slid over the console and spilled onto the pavement, pistol already up, she took cover behind the axle and peppered a pair of boots that tried to flank.

“Visual on Maera,” Alice snapped into comms, breathless. “Ten total on lead push; five down; three circling left; two on rear.”

“Copy,” Rio said, wiping blood from her eyebrow with her sleeve. She grabbed a dropped baton, weighed it once, then changed her grip to the short end.

Three mercs broke to her left, trying to torch the SUV with a compact accelerant canister. Rio moved before they did, two steps, a feint right, then she slung the baton like a thrown hammer. It cracked the canister holder in the wrist; flame licked across his sleeve and he dropped it with a scream. She dove on the device, booted it under the ramming vehicle, and fired a single round to ignite it. A quick belly of orange whoofed under the chassis, forcing two would-be flankers to leap back, cursing.

Something dark flickered to her right. She turned too late. A baton whistled and clipped her ribs where they already ground. White pain flared; she tasted metal. She absorbed it, stepped into the next strike, caught the attacker’s wrist against her forearm, and let the momentum carry his arm high. Her knee pistoned up into his liver twice; he folded, gagging. She drove an elbow into the base of his skull to finish him. Another swung for her temple; she let gravity drop her under it, rolled, and came up with a broken mirror shard in her fist, raking it across the attacker’s Achilles tendon, dropping him screaming onto one knee. She hammered him out.

“Nat?” Rio called, breath sawing.

A groan from the driver’s seat. “Pinned,” Natasha ground out, voice paper-thin. “Left ankle… door’s kissing it. Clint—” A cough. “Alive. Concussed.”

“Copy. Keep your head down.”

The crowd murmured, pulled backward by instinct and fascination. Rio clocked their phones, their line of sight, the angles. She moved between Agatha and glass and guns without thinking, always reclaiming centerline. Agatha’s eyes, wide and bright with terror, tracked her like a lifeline.

Maera eased forward, letting her mercs spend themselves. She cocked her head, studying Rio’s feet, the set of her shoulders, the way her off-hand flexed and reset around the pistol grip.

“You move like a Ranger,” Maera called, conversational over the chaos. “I’ve killed a few of those.”

“You won’t kill this one,” Rio said.

Maera smiled without warmth. “You already failed one lover. The odds aren’t with you.”

Rage pricked the edges of Rio’s vision, sharp and clarifying. She did not answer. She did not rise to it. She just breathed and moved.

Two more came fast from opposite directions. She stepped to split them, ate a punch to give one back twice as hard. The second went low for a tackle; she sprawled, hooked his chin with her forearm and torqued, dumping him. She snatched his sidearm mid-fall and fired two rounds into the attacker behind him, then stitched one into the left-side runner’s thigh to stop his movement. A third merc lunged from the periphery; she pivoted and fired, but the slide locked back on empty. The merc grinned and raised his gun.

The shot never came. Bucky’s round took him in the ear. He spun, dropped boneless. “Move, Vidal!” Bucky shouted, already turning to engage the two trying to flank Yelena.

“On you!” Yelena yelled to Bucky, charging through the narrow aisle of space the crowd left, a wicked grin carved across her blood-slick face. She vaulted the hood of an abandoned coupe, tackled a merc into a sandwich board, snapped a zip-tie around his wrists with the kind of feral efficiency that said she’d done this a thousand times.

Alice, crouched, fired a short, angry burst that cracked a windshield and made two more take cover behind a mailbox. “Two left on my side, one trying for angle on Agatha!”

Rio didn’t look. She felt it, the shift, the aim. She ripped the rear door of the wrecked SUV open, yanked the fallen driver’s long baton free from the floorboard, and whirled it once. The merc peeking around the mailbox lined his sights on Agatha’s door window. Rio snapped her wrist and hurled the baton like a spear. It spun end-over-end and smashed the gun out of his hands; his finger reflex-fired; the shot went wild into the air.

Silence opened at the edges, the kind that happens before the final storm. Only Maera stood between Rio and the broken semicircle of men and women groaning on the pavement. Sirens had grown close now, a red-blue flicker washing the storefronts. Civilians filmed, spellbound.

Maera’s pistol came up, steady as a sermon. She fired twice.

The first round caught Rio high and back of the shoulder, spinning her, ripping hot pain through muscle. The second punched through her side, nasty and low. Air left her lungs in a rough bark. She staggered, a knee touching down as the world went white around the edges.

Agatha screamed, a raw, animal sound, clawing at the jammed seatbelt. “Río!”

Bucky broke left, trying to cut the angle, but a remaining merc lunged for his ankles. He booted the man in the face and kept moving, but the second’s delay cost him three steps he needed.

Maera didn’t advance. She closed the distance with words, not feet. “You’re bleeding out,” she said, voice almost kind. “How many ghosts are staring at you now? That pretty Valkyrie. Your team. Billy. Think they’ll make room for an actress?”

Rio planted a boot, forced herself upright. Every nerve burned. Blood soaked into her waistband, sticky and hot. She tuned the pain down the way she’d been taught, made it a radio she could turn to a murmur. She kept her gun hand steady by locking her other hand around her wrist, arms stiff, stance narrow to protect the hole in her side.

“Say her name again,” she hissed, “and I’ll make sure you don’t die clean.”

Maera smirked. “I don’t plan on dying at all.”

She came in fast, like a coiled spring unleashed, two shots in quick succession, both aimed center mass where she’d seen blood spreading. Rio didn’t step back; she stepped sideways hard, felt the first round tear through her chest and the second graze the meat of her ribs, but she ignored it, and snapped her muzzle up toward Maera’s face.

Maera swatted the barrel aside, grabbed Rio’s wrist, and jammed her thumb into the wound at Rio’s side. White-hot agony detonated. Rio’s vision blew to static. Maera used the opening to slam her forearm across Rio’s throat and drive her backwards into the SUV’s bent frame. Metal bit into Rio’s spine. The pistol skittered from her hand.

Agatha was half out of the car now, fingers on the ruined door, eyes wild. “Stop! Please!”

“Stay back!” Bucky roared, catching her around the waist and hauling her off the seat before she toppled onto broken glass. He wrapped her in both arms, pinning her, as she thrashed. “You can’t—Agatha, you can’t!”

Rio’s hands found Maera’s jaw and ear, hunting for pain points. Maera kept her base low, hips heavy, forearm crushing. Black sparkles ate at the edges of Rio’s view. She palmed Maera’s eye, shoved; Maera snarled and bit her palm, a harsh, brutal chomp that broke skin. Rio drove a knee up and sideways, catching Maera’s kidney. It bought an inch. She took it, twisted under the forearm, got her shoulder low, and used the wrecked door frame to lever herself free.

They separated, two steps, three, both breathing hard. Maera’s brow was split; blood crawled into one eye. Rio’s side wept freely now; every inhale tasted like iron.

“Fun,” Maera panted. She smiled, feral. “Let’s finish.”

She holstered the pistol. That surprised Rio. Then Maera drew a fighting knife from the small of her back, broad, ugly, single-edged, and spun it once in her hand.

Rio didn’t have a blade. She glanced down: glass, a bent license plate, a length of snapped steel wiper arm. She snatched the wiper arm, weighed it, awkward, but it had reach, and settled into a low guard.

Maera stepped in, testing, slash high to make Rio block, feint low to see if she’d bite. Rio didn’t. She batted the first cut aside, felt the knife kiss the air an inch from her cheek, then jabbed the tip of the wiper at Maera’s throat. Maera parried it with the knife’s spine, rolled her wrist, and nicked Rio’s forearm hard enough to sting.

Another trade, Rio lunged to the outside of Maera’s knife hand, trying to control the elbow; Maera rotated out, cutting a line across Rio’s hip. Rio bit the pain in half and answered with a crack across Maera’s temple with the wiper arm. Maera reeled, then came back twice as fast, a furious flurry, slash, slash, thrust. Rio gave ground, boot soles slipping on glass, parrying with both hands until the wiper arm bent in the middle with a sad squeal.

Maera grinned. “You’re breaking.”

“So are you,” Rio said, and head-butted her.

Bone met bone. The shock traveled down Rio’s neck and lit up the place where the bullet sat, but Maera’s nose exploded, a red gush. She stumbled. Rio tried to capitalize, swinging the wiper in a low arc at Maera’s knee. Maera jumped the sweep, planted, and drove the knife up under Rio’s ribs, but Rio twisted at the last instant so the blade shaved along ribs instead of puncturing deep. It still set her world on fire.

Agatha’s voice came through the static, torn and wrecked. “Río, please—please—”

“Watch me,” Rio whispered under her breath. She let the wiper arm drop, feinted as if the pain had stolen her balance, and let Maera come in greedy for the finish.

Maera took the bait. She stepped deep on Rio’s lead foot, knife angling for the notch above the hip. At the last instant, Rio pivoted on her heel, caught Maera’s knife wrist with both hands, and slammed it into the SUV’s bent door seam. Maera cursed and tried to rip free.

Rio let go of the wrist and went for the gun. Her pistol lay a few feet away, just past a glittering fan of safety glass. She dove, ribs screaming, and grabbed it left-handed because the right shook too much. Maera tore the knife free with a shriek of twisted steel and spun, raising the blade.

They moved at the same time, Maera driving in, knife flashing; Rio rolling to her back, bracing her elbows, bringing the muzzle up one-handed.

Two shots cracked almost as one, but only one found its mark where it mattered.

The round took Maera high between the eyes. Her expression froze mid-snarl. She dropped like a marionette with cut strings, knife thudding into the street a foot from Rio’s ear.

Everything went quiet then. Not truly, the sirens were on top of them now, people were screaming, someone was sobbing, but inside the circle of Rio’s breath, there was a hollow calm.

Her gun fell from her fingers. She tried to sit up, and the world tilted. She got as far as a lean on one elbow before the street rose to meet her. She rolled to her side to keep from choking on the iron in her mouth and pressed her palm to her wound. Warmth pulsed between her fingers.

“Rio!” Agatha was half out of the wreck, only Bucky’s iron arms keeping her from collapsing face-first onto the asphalt. She fought him, wild. “Let me go! Let me go!”

“Stay behind me,” he snarled, dragging her away from a fallen pistol someone’s shoe nudged toward them in the chaos. He wrapped her in a bear hug and physically carried her to the curb, setting her down only when she was shielded by the engine block.

Natasha stirred, a raw groan leaking past clenched teeth. She reached for the dash, found the door handle with searching fingers, and tried to lever herself free; her foot was tangled under warped metal. “Clint?” she rasped.

He answered with a low, pained grunt and a flutter of eyelashes.

Yelena limped into view, a gash across her hairline painting half her face red, eyes bright and murderous. “Who’s next?” she demanded of nobody, knife still in hand. Alice flanked her, gun tight, scanning every rooftop.

“Clear!” Bucky shouted, pivoting, gun out. “Clear left!”

“Right clear!” Yelena snapped, voice hard. “Downed hostiles only!”

Rio heard them with half an ear. Her world had narrowed to the square of sky between two buildings and the sound of Agatha sobbing her name into Bucky’s shoulder. The rest was drowned out, sirens, shouts, boots scuffing pavement. Even her own heartbeat, usually so steady, was broken glass in her ears, jagged and fading.

She wanted to lift her head, tell Agatha she was fine, but her body refused. Four gunshots burned like firebrands, her shoulder useless, her rib shattered, her lung drowning her in every breath. Three knives still seemed lodged in phantom places: her abdomen, her side, her hip. She couldn’t feel her left hand anymore. Her ribs grated like broken wood.

And then nothing.

Her chest stilled.

“She’s not breathing!” a paramedic shouted, already vaulting over the curb.

Agatha tore herself from Bucky’s arms, throwing herself onto the pavement beside Rio. Her hands fluttered uselessly, bloody fingers trying to press against wounds she couldn’t close. “No, no, no, please—” Her voice cracked on every syllable.

“Get her back!” another medic barked. CPR started immediately. The sound was sickening—bones that hadn’t broken yet cracking under the force of compressions.

“One, two, three—”

Agatha clawed at the medic’s arm. “You’re hurting her!”

“She’s gone if we stop!” the medic snapped.

Rio’s head lolled to one side, lips bluish, blood seeping steadily from her chest wound.

“Charge paddles!”

“No time, we keep compressions!”

Bucky grabbed Agatha’s shoulders, dragging her back against him as she tried to lunge forward again. She fought him like a wildcat, kicking, screaming. “She’s all I have! Don’t you stop! Don’t you dare stop!”

Yelena was on her knees, hands pressed into Rio’s abdomen, covered to the elbows in her blood, snarling through gritted teeth. “She doesn’t get to die! Do you hear me, Vidal? Not after all this shit!”

Alice’s voice was shaking as she counted out compressions for the medic, trying to keep her brain on rhythm instead of the horror. “Twenty-seven, twenty-eight, twenty-nine—”

Rio’s body jolted under the weight of their efforts.

The medic sealed an oxygen mask to her face, squeezing the bag, but the chest remained stubbornly still.

“Flatline,” came the grim call.

Agatha’s scream was ragged, inhuman.

“Again!” another medic shouted. Compressions redoubled.

One second. Two.

And then a cough. Wet, rattling, but a cough.

Her chest hitched, shallow but there.

“She’s back, we have a pulse! Move, move!”

They loaded her onto a gurney with brutal efficiency. IVs in both arms. Pressure bandages stacked thick over her wounds. One medic straddled the gurney, continuing compressions as they wheeled her toward the ambulance.

Agatha ran alongside, tripping, half-carried by Alice and Bucky. “I’m coming with her, you hear me?!”

“You can’t!” a medic snapped. “She’s critical, we need space to work!”

“You’ll have to shoot me to stop me!” Agatha screamed back, eyes wild, streaked with mascara and blood.

Rio’s chest fluttered once, then stopped again.

“She’s flatlining! Clear!”

They slammed paddles onto her chest, the shock snapping her body upward. No rhythm. Another jolt. No rhythm.

Agatha sobbed into Bucky’s arms, shaking uncontrollably. “She promised me she wouldn’t leave me!”

On the third shock, a weak blip appeared on the monitor. The medic exhaled hard. “We’ve got her, barely. Load her up!”

The ambulance doors slammed shut, red lights flaring.

At the hospital, chaos reigned. Nurses shouted vitals, doctors barked orders, carts rattled. Rio’s body was barely recognizable under the tubes, wires, and blood-soaked bandages.

“She’s crashing again—push epi!”

“BP’s bottoming out—”

“Lung collapsed—needle, now!”

Agatha clung to the glass outside the trauma bay, pounding with her fists. “She’s mine! Don’t let her die!”

Yelena tried to pull her back, but Agatha spun, feral. “You don’t understand, I didn’t get to tell her—” Her voice broke. “Not enough. Not like I should’ve.”

Inside, Rio’s body arched with another shock, monitor squealing. Flatline again.

Agatha dropped to her knees, screaming into her hands.

And still the team worked, refusing to give her up.

Chapter End Notes

Only 3-4 more chapters left. Hang in there.

Where Healing Begins

Chapter Summary

“Scars remind us where we’ve been. They don’t have to dictate where we’re going.” – David Rossi

Chapter Notes

*Peeks out from behind Riot Shield* No one panic, I will not be killing off Rio or any Lesbians in this story, we've been through enough already. I just had to make you sweat a little bit because I am evil.

See the end of the chapter for more notes

The hospital waiting room was too bright, too sterile. Its beige walls and buzzing lights pressed down on everyone like a weight. Agatha sat in the corner, hunched forward in one of the stiff vinyl chairs, elbows on her knees, her hands trembling. Wanda was at her side, a steady anchor, but her face was pale and drawn tight. Lilia had both hands wrapped around a paper cup of untouched coffee, staring into it like it might give her answers. Jen paced in a straight line, muttering under her breath, her phone clutched in her hand.

And then there was the team.

Natasha stood with her arms crossed, her jaw clenched so tightly it looked like stone. Yelena had worn a hole into the tile with how many times she’d circled the room, muttering in Russian curses too sharp to translate. Bucky leaned against the far wall, one hand curled into a fist, the other pinching the bridge of his nose like he was barely holding himself together. Clint sat slouched but coiled, eyes darting to every doorway like a hawk ready to launch. Alice had her laptop open, but her eyes weren’t on the screen; they were blank, unseeing, her mind clearly elsewhere.

They were wolves caged in glass, every muscle taut, every breath edged in fury and helplessness.

Hours stretched and folded in on themselves. The clock ticked too loudly. Every time someone in scrubs walked by, Agatha’s head jerked up, hope flashing, only to collapse again when it wasn’t their doctor.

Finally, Maria Hill arrived. Her heels clicked across the tile, her face grave. She moved with the kind of composure Agatha had come to associate with Rio: professional, impenetrable, controlled. But Agatha caught the flicker in Maria’s eyes when they scanned the room, the grief, the fury, the fear, and knew she was holding herself together by sheer will.

Maria pulled her phone from her pocket and exhaled sharply. “The world already knows.”

“What?” Jen snapped.

Maria turned the screen to show them. Videos. Dozens of them. Clips from cell phones: civilians screaming as two SUVs were slammed off the road, as armed men dragged themselves out, as gunfire cracked down a city street. A shaky zoomed-in video of Rio fighting alone, blood soaking her shirt, blades flashing in her hands. The final duel with Maera, Rio’s staggering but unrelenting fight until the final headshot.

The footage was everywhere.

Agatha slapped a hand over her mouth, the sound of Rio’s ragged breaths on screen tearing her apart.

“They’re calling it the ‘Battle of Sunset Strip,’” Maria said grimly. “The footage is being played on every network. Headlines are speculating. We can’t stay quiet, not after this. If we don’t say something, the world will write its own story.”

“What do we tell them?” Clint asked, his voice low, dark.

Maria’s eyes swept the group, lingering longer on her wife for a few extra seconds, but finally settled on Agatha, softening just slightly. “We tell them the truth. That Rio survived the ambush. That she’s in critical condition. That she’s fighting.”

“No,” Agatha rasped, shaking her head furiously. Her voice cracked, raw. “You can’t—don’t tell them how bad it is. If she—if she doesn’t—” Her words choked into silence.

“Agatha,” Maria said gently but firmly. “The footage already shows the world how bad it is. We don’t control that. But what we can control is the message. We give the world something to rally around, something to hold onto. And Rio deserves for them to know how hard she fought.”

Agatha squeezed her hands into fists until her nails bit skin. Tears burned, but she nodded, because what else could she do?

Maria typed, her words measured, steady. Out loud, she read:

“S.H.I.E.L.D. can confirm that our co-founder, Rio Vidal, was critically injured during an ambush on Sunset Strip. Despite being outnumbered more than ten to one, she neutralized Maera Rourke, the Seventh Veil key enforcer, and more than two dozen mercenaries. She suffered multiple life-threatening injuries in the process and is currently in critical condition. She is receiving the best care possible, and we ask for privacy and respect as she continues to fight. Rio Vidal has always been relentless in her protection of others. We believe in her, and we stand with her.”

-Co CEO and Founder of S.H.I.E.L.D. Maria Romanov-Hill

She hit send before anyone could argue.

Silence swallowed the room.

Agatha pressed her face into her hands, muffling the sob that broke out of her chest. Wanda leaned against her, her own eyes wet. Lilia slid the untouched coffee aside and reached across the table, her hand shaking as she covered Agatha’s.

Then Jen’s phone buzzed. And Alice’s laptop pinged. And Clint’s own device lit up.

The internet had exploded.

@WitchyAnon: She fought alone against two dozen mercs. I can’t believe this is real life. #PrayForRio #ProtectAgatha

@BelowDeckStan: That video… Rio took on a warzone by herself. And she won. Hero doesn’t even cover it.

@CovenHeart89: No words. Just praying for Rio. Protectors like her are one in a billion.

@SaltLakeCynic: The fact that she was stabbed, shot, and still killed Maera?? Hollywood has nothing on this woman. #RioVidal

@ThirstyForSecurity: Me: crying. Also me: damn Rio looks hot even while fighting for her life. She'd better pull through.

@Agatha_Updates: The Fact that Rio got up every time and positioned herself in front of Agatha, and Agatha is screaming for Rio in the back. My heart breaks, #Pray4Rio #Agathario

CNN: “Unprecedented: Security CEO Defends Actress in Violent Ambush”

Variety: “The Battle of Sunset Strip: Rio Vidal Hospitalized After Fatal Standoff”

BBC: “Who is Rio Vidal? The Ex-Special Forces Bodyguard Who Took Down Maera Rourke”

Rolling Stone: “From Shadow to Spotlight: Rio Vidal’s Legendary Stand”

Agatha stared at the headlines through bleary eyes, feeling detached, like she was floating outside herself. None of it mattered. Only Rio mattered. Only the fact that she was behind those doors, maybe slipping away with every tick of the clock.

More hours passed. Nurses came and went. Food was delivered but untouched. Every sound from the trauma bay made Agatha’s heart seize.

Finally, the doors opened. A doctor in pale blue scrubs stepped out, peeling off his gloves. He looked exhausted, his cap soaked with sweat.

Everyone in the waiting room shot to their feet.

“Ms. Vidal?” the doctor asked.

Agatha stumbled forward, Wanda catching her elbow. “Yes—she—”

The doctor softened. “She’s alive.”

The words cracked through the tension like lightning. Agatha sagged, nearly collapsing, but Wanda and Lilia caught her.

“She is alive,” the doctor repeated firmly, making sure it sank in. “We had to perform extensive surgery. Four gunshot wounds, three stab wounds, multiple fractures, collapsed lung, severe internal bleeding. It was touch-and-go. She flatlined several times, but we brought her back each time.”

Agatha clutched the doctor’s sleeve. “Can I see her?”

“You can, but…” His face sobered. “She’s in a medically induced coma. Her body needs time to recover. We’ll monitor her closely. The next seventy-two hours will be critical. But for now, she’s stable.”

Agatha’s tears spilled hot and fast. Relief, grief, terror, all at once. Wanda wrapped her in a fierce hug, Lilia’s hand pressed against her back, Jen dabbing her own eyes.

Behind them, the team exhaled in unison, the weight of hours lifted but not gone. Natasha hugged Maria tightly, Yelena swiped furiously at her eyes, Clint sat down heavily as if his knees had given out, and Bucky muttered something low that might’ve been a prayer.

Agatha could only whisper, over and over: “She’s alive. She’s alive. She’s alive.”

The words tasted like salvation and fear all at once.

The room was all hush and hum, soft machines, measured beeps, the gentle rasp of the ventilator pushing air into lungs that had fought too hard for too long. Rio lay so still beneath the thin hospital blanket that for a moment Agatha forgot how to breathe herself. She swallowed, eased closer, and wrapped her fingers around Rio’s hand, warm, heavy, flecked with old scars and fresh tape. There were bruises flowering beneath the IV, gauze peeking from under the sleeve of the hospital gown, a faint smear of antiseptic scent threaded through the sterile air.

“Hi, darling,” Agatha whispered, because anything louder felt like it might shatter the fragile bubble keeping Rio tethered here. “It’s me.”

The monitors continued their steady negotiations with fate. Agatha’s thumb traced the back of Rio’s knuckles, over a small ridge of bone that never healed perfectly, over the tendons that always flexed when Rio laughed. She brought those fingers to her lips and kissed them, tears already stinging.

“You’re stubborn,” she said, trying to smile. “So don’t you dare decide now is the moment to try something new.”

The door sighed open. Natasha stepped in first, shoulders squared, face composed, eyes red-rimmed but dry. Maria was beside her, hand brushing Natasha’s back in a touch so light it could have been breath. Lilia followed, her movements careful, like she was afraid of waking the pain. Behind them came Yelena, Bucky, Clint, and Alice, each quieter than Agatha had ever seen.

No one spoke for a long moment. They just gathered around the bed, their bodies a wall in a world that had already thrown everything at them.

Natasha finally stepped forward, stopping at the foot of the bed. “She looks better than I expected,” she said softly.

“She looks very angry to still be here,” Yelena muttered, swallowing hard. “Good. Anger keeps you alive.”

Bucky moved to the opposite side of the bed, eyes fixed on Rio’s face like he was counting every freckle. “You did it, kid,” he said, voice gravelly. “You kept her breathing. Now it’s your turn.”

Clint scrubbed a hand over his jaw and exhaled. “I owe you fifty bucks for that last shot. You still land cleaner headshots than me, and I’m offended.”

Alice stood at the end of the bed, hands tucked into her hoodie pocket. “I wish I’d been there in time,” she said. “I’m sorry, Rio. I’ll make it up to you. I’ll find the rest.”

Agatha squeezed Rio’s hand gently. “You hear that?” she whispered to her. “Your family’s here.”

Maria came closer and set two fingers on Rio’s forearm, like a grounding wire. “You’re allowed to sleep,” she murmured. “But you’re not allowed to leave.”

Natasha’s mouth wobbled and steadied. “We’ll take your shift,” she said to the quiet machines. “You rest.”

They each spoke in their way. A sentence. A small story. A promise. Clint promised to fix the squeaky door in the house that's been bothering her. Bucky promised to bully the physical therapist on Rio’s behalf when the time came. Yelena promised to “kill everyone twice” if they even breathed in Agatha’s direction. Maria didn’t promise. She simply said, “I’ve got you,” like she had a thousand times since the first day they’d met.

When the room had absorbed their words, Agatha drew a breath and stood. “Thank you,” she said, voice steadying by force. “Now I need you to do something for me.”

Every head turned.

“Go home.” Agatha lifted a hand when they started to protest. “All of you. Go home. Call your people. Crawl into bed with them. Eat real food. Shower. Sleep. You’ve been here since yesterday, and there’s nothing more you can do tonight that you haven’t already done.” She glanced at Maria and Natasha. “Except you two. You’re staying. And Lilia.”

“No,” Yelena said instantly, shaking her head. “I’m not—”

Agatha reached across Rio’s torso and caught Yelena’s hand. “Please. For me. You’ll come back in the morning and change places with Natasha and Maria. We are not falling apart one by one because none of us slept. That’s not what Rio would want.”

It was the appeal that landed. Even in her silence, Rio’s command still lived here.

Bucky nodded first. “I’ll take Yelena and Clint,” he said, not looking thrilled about it. “Alice, you coming?”

Alice’s jaw worked. She looked like the word no was latched to her teeth. Then she saw the pleading in Agatha’s face. “I’ll go,” she whispered. “But I’m setting alerts. If anything changes, we’re back in fifteen.”

“Ten,” Yelena countered.

“Seven,” Clint said dryly, because he couldn’t help himself.

They each touched Rio before they left, an old ritual, a new prayer. A squeeze to her shoulder. The lightest brush of knuckles against her hairline. Yelena bent and kissed her temple, quick and fierce, then glanced away as if embarrassed by tenderness. Bucky pressed his fist gently to Rio’s arm like a soldier acknowledging another soldier. Clint muttered, “You scary, terrifying woman. Don’t you dare make me cry again.” Alice tucked the blanket more neatly along Rio’s side, the way she always straightened her gear before a mission.

When the door closed behind them, the room exhaled. Quieter still. The beeps seemed softer. The air warmer.

Maria moved a chair to the other side of the bed. Natasha didn’t sit. She hovered at the footboard, sentinel. Lilia took her place near the window, the amber city glow washing her face in melancholy light. She clasped her hands tightly in her lap like she might wring water from grief if she twisted hard enough.

Agatha rested her forehead on the back of Rio’s hand and just breathed. After a while, she spoke without lifting it. “When she fell,” she said, “I thought—” Her breath hitched. “I thought I was hearing the sound of my own heart tearing apart. And then she stopped breathing and I—”

Natasha came around the bed then, quiet as fog, and placed a hand between Agatha’s shoulders. “She’s here,” she said simply. “You did what you had to. And she did what she always does.”

Agatha finally looked up. “She always does too much,” she said, trying to be wry and failing. “She doesn’t know where the line is.”

“She knows,” Maria said. “She just runs through it.”

That landed in Agatha’s ribs like a bell. She kissed Rio’s fingers again.

Lilia shifted, scooting her chair closer to the foot of the bed. “I never thanked you properly,” she said, soft but clear, looking at Rio’s unconscious face. “For bringing my boy home the first time.” She swallowed, eyes glistening. “I wanted to hate you when those photos leaked. Not because of you, because of the memories. But I can’t. Colten loved you. He admired you. And I see now why.”

Agatha reached her free hand toward Lilia; Lilia took it. They held on to each other across the sheets and wires.

The clock ticked toward a late, unkind hour. Nurses came and went, their movements gentle. A doctor poked his head in, nodded at Maria, glanced at the readings, and left. The ventilator sighed. Rio’s chest rose and fell. Sometimes her fingers twitched beneath Agatha’s thumb, phantom echoes of a fight still happening somewhere inside her systems.

Agatha leaned in and spoke softly, just for Rio. “Do you remember the first time you cooked for me? You rolled your eyes at my pizza order and then made me the best coffee I’ve ever had in my life. I thought: this is what safety tastes like.” Her voice trembled and steadied. “You told me once you were afraid you couldn’t bear it if someone you loved died and you couldn’t stop it. And I told you I didn’t need a hero, I needed you. I still do. I need you to come back and be annoying about my sleep schedule and tell me to drink water and hide the spray bottle from Jen.”

Natasha snorted softly. “She hid it in the glovebox last week.”

Agatha’s mouth quirked. “Of course she did.”

Agatha’s eyes filled again. She focused on a single freckle on Rio’s cheek and spoke to it like it might reply. “I was going to tell you back at the cabin,” she whispered. “I love you. I said it before, but I need to say it again. I love you, Rio Vidal. Please don’t make me say it to a grave.”

Silence fell heavy and tender. The machine beeps kept time with the ache.

After a while, Natasha said, “Do you want us to step out? Give you a minute?”

Agatha shook her head and squeezed Rio’s hand. “No. Stay.” She glanced at the chairs. “But you should sit. I’m not moving.”

Maria sat. Natasha perched at last, only because Maria tugged her down beside her. Lilia drew her knees up and rested her chin there like a girl remembering a different life.

They talked quietly, the way people talk when someone sleeps a foot away. Not about tactics or bounties or the Snake. In this room, those names held no power.

They spoke about Rio. About the time, she fixed a jammed camera crane on set with two hairpins and a curse. About how she pretended not to like Below Deck but texted Agatha an analysis of the cast’s conflict arcs at three in the morning. About the tattoo on her ribs that almost no one had seen up close, the tiny linework of a raven in flight, inked on the day she met Maria and Natasha, because she said they felt like the first wings she grew back after the war took them.

Agatha held those stories like talismans, turning each one over in her heart, polishing them with memory until they gleamed.

A nurse slipped in to check the lines and levels. “She’s holding steady,” the nurse murmured, and Agatha could have kissed her shoes.

Maria stood then, stretched out the knot in her back, and crossed to Agatha. “You should eat,” she said. “Or at least drink something.”

“I can’t,” Agatha said simply.

“You can,” Maria corrected gently. “But not yet. When you can’t, we will.”

Agatha nodded. The gratitude hurt. “Thank you for staying.”

“We’re exactly where we’re supposed to be,” Natasha said. She rested her palms on the end of the bed. “We’ll rotate. Yelena and Bucky will be back at dawn. Clint and Alice will take the midday. You won’t be alone here a single second.”

Agatha looked down at Rio’s face, calm in its strange, borrowed sleep. “I should have insisted on an armored motorcade,” she whispered, guilt’s old talons digging in. “I should have—”

“Stop,” Natasha said, simple and firm. “We make plans. The enemy makes plans. What met on that street was not your fault.”

“She’s right,” Maria said. “Rio chose. She always chooses. If she could speak right now, she’d tell you to stop stealing her agency with your guilt.”

That startled a laugh out of Agatha. “God, she would.”

“‘Stop being dramatic, Harkness,’” Natasha imitated, perfectly dry.

“‘Drink water, Harkness,’” Lilia added, rolling her eyes fondly, and even Agatha smiled properly then.

For a while, the room settled into a fragile peace. Lilia dozed in her chair, chin tucked to her chest. Natasha and Maria murmured to each other in that seamless shorthand of people who have done life and war and laundry together. Agatha kept her post, one hand around Rio’s, the other carding gently through her hair, tracing the curl behind her ear that never stayed put.

Agatha leaned closer, nose almost brushing Rio’s cheek. “Do you know,” she whispered, “that the first time I watched you laugh, really laugh, I thought—oh, I’m in trouble. I’ll never get over that sound.” Her fingers trembled in Rio’s hair. “So you can’t go anywhere. I’m greedy. I want that sound for a long, long time.”

The ventilator sighed. The heart monitor tapped out its measured reply.

Agatha lowered her forehead to the back of Rio’s hand again and closed her eyes. “Sleep,” she said. “I’ve got you.”

Minutes stretched. Or hours. Time lost its meaning in the steady exchange of machine and breath, in the warm weight of Rio’s hand against her lips.

The door nudged open once more. A different doctor this time, older, careful-eyed. He checked the readings, glanced over the incisions that peeked just above the gown’s edge, and nodded with quiet satisfaction. “She’s stable,” he said, keeping his voice low. “No new bleeding. We’ll keep her under tonight to give her body a fighting chance to repair. If this holds, we’ll reassess in the morning.”

“Can she hear us?” Agatha asked, the question catching at her throat.

“Sometimes,” the doctor said. “Sometimes the body hears, even if the mind is far away.” He offered a small smile. “Keep talking.”

“Always,” Agatha said.

When he left, Maria checked the time. “It’s nearly three,” she murmured. “Try to close your eyes, Agatha. Natasha and I will keep watch.”

Agatha shook her head. “I’ll close them when she opens hers.”

Lilia stirred and blinked at the window. “The city’s going to wake up soon,” she whispered, like a warning and a promise. “It will have opinions again.”

“They can shout into the void,” Natasha said, unbothered. “We’ll be here.”

Agatha bowed her head and pressed a final kiss to Rio’s knuckles for the hundredth time since she’d walked into the room. “I love you,” she said, not caring who heard, not anymore. “Come back to me.”

Outside the window, the sky lightened from black to bruised blue. Inside, the machines kept their patient vigil. Maria and Natasha settled into their posts, two sentinels carved from steel and grief. Lilia watched the horizon and breathed. Agatha threaded her fingers through Rio’s and refused to let go.

For now, the world was held together by a handful of women in a too-bright room and the steady, stubborn beat of a heart that still, miraculously, ferociously refused to stop.

 

The third sunrise broke pale and patient across the hospital blinds when Rio’s fingers flexed beneath Agatha’s. It was the smallest thing, a feather twitch, but it sent electricity through Agatha’s whole body. She jerked upright, sleep-deprived eyes flying to Rio’s face.

“Sweetheart?” Her voice cracked. “Rio, can you hear me?”

For a beat, the room was only machines: the soft hush of oxygen through the nasal cannula (they’d weaned her off the ventilator during the night), the reliable metronome of the heart monitor. Then Rio’s eyelashes trembled. Her brow knit, like she was trying to read a map in the dark. Her lips parted on a breath that sounded like gravel dragged over glass.

Agatha squeezed gently, trying to anchor her. “You’re safe. You’re with me.”

Rio’s eyes opened, slow, unfocused, green-gold searching for purchase. They landed on Agatha’s face, and her whole body seemed to loosen. She tried to smile; it came out lopsided and a little dazed.

“Hey,” Agatha whispered, laughing and crying in the same breath. “Hi. There you are.”

Rio tried to say something. What came out was a broken rasp. She swallowed, winced, tried again. “You… okay?”

Agatha laughed wetly, wiped her cheeks with the heel of her hand, and leaned in to press the gentlest kiss to Rio’s forehead, careful of bruises and tape. “Am I okay? You got shot four times. You were stabbed three times.” Her voice wobbled. “I’m okay. You’re the one who—”

“Alive,” Rio croaked, a ghost of a grin. “Told you… stubborn.”

Agatha dropped her forehead to their joined hands and just breathed. Relief swamped her so hard it left her shaky. “I am never letting you live this down.”

The door swished open. A nurse stepped in, took one look at Agatha’s face, then at Rio’s open eyes, and broke into a grin. “Well, hello there, Miss Vidal,” she said softly. “Welcome back.”

The next ten minutes were a slow blur of gentle checks and questions. Can you wiggle your toes? Squeeze my fingers? On a scale of one to ten, how’s your pain? Rio answered with the gritty patience of someone used to being stitched back together and graded on resilience. When they asked if she knew where she was, she croaked, “Hospital. Los Angeles. Year’s… the same?” The nurse laughed. “Close enough.”

The nurse promised to alert the attending and slipped out. Agatha brushed damp curls off Rio’s temple and tried to memorize every millimeter of her face as if seeing it for the first time again. “You scared me,” she said, voice small in the sudden quiet. “You scared all of us.”

Rio blinked slowly. “I’m sorry.” She shifted, a stab of pain tugging the corner of her mouth. “The team…?” Her eyes flicked, fear, guilt, calculation, all the reflexes of a protector inventorying casualties.

Agatha knew what she was asking. Her throat thickened, but she forced the words out, steady for Rio’s sake. “Natasha’s got a sprained wrist and ankle, and a broken rib. Yelena’s walking around with a bruised rib and a handful of cuts. Clint’s concussed, split his head open, but the doctors say he’ll heal. Bucky and Alice escaped mostly unharmed, just a few scrapes from broken glass.”

She paused, her voice taking on a colder tone. “Maera’s dead.”

Rio blinked slowly, her lashes heavy against her cheeks. For a moment, silence hung in the sterile air, punctuated only by the steady beep of the heart monitor. Then, with effort, she rasped, “You?”

Her head twitched slightly, as though she was trying to turn toward Agatha, but the stitches pulled at her skin and drew a sharp wince.

Agatha immediately leaned closer, her hand already tightening around Rio’s. “I’m okay,” she whispered, voice thick with the tears she was holding back. “Just a few cuts from the glass, and a few bruises, nothing more. You—” her voice trembled now, unable to stay even, “you saved me. Yet again.”

Her eyes glistened, the sheen of unshed tears catching the low light of the hospital room. She lifted Rio’s hand, pressing it against her own cheek as though needing to prove she was still there, still whole.

Rio stared at the ceiling for a hard beat, something like relief sparking through her eyes.

A knock brushed the door. Maria leaned in first, hair pulled back, eyes exhausted and luminous. Natasha was half a step behind, composed as ever, but when she saw Rio awake, the composure cracked. She exhaled like she’d been holding her breath underwater for three days.

“You look terrible,” Natasha said, which was Widow-speak for thank God. “Don’t do that again.”

Rio’s laugh rasped. “I’ll… pencil it in.”

Maria came to the bedside and took the free hand that wasn’t glued to Natasha’s. Her thumb stroked across Rio’s knuckles, slow and grounding. “Welcome back, kid,” she said, voice rough. “You did well.”

“Team?” Rio murmured.

“All here,” Maria said. “And waiting outside like caffeinated wolves.”

As if summoned, Yelena’s voice carried from the hallway. “If she’s awake and you do not allow me to bully her about physical therapy immediately, I will burn this hospital down.”

“Door stays intact,” the nurse warned from the threshold, amused. “Two at a time, though please,”

They piled in anyway, Bucky, Yelena, and Clint push each other to be the first ones through the door, Alice comes in clutching a bouquet of wildly incongruent gas-station flowers like they were contraband. Lilia followed, eyes already shining. Jen slipped in behind, trying (failing) to hide that she’d been crying mascara tracks. Wanda eased last, a quiet storm in a black coat, Vision and Tommy just outside the door. Wanda caught Agatha’s eye and nodded: Later. I’m here.

For a moment, no one spoke. They stood around the bed, a mixture of love and exasperation and terror-turned-relief.

Yelena planted her hands on her hips. “You are very dramatic, yes?” she said to Rio. “There are easier ways to get attention than stopping your heart.”

Rio’s mouth curved. “Says the woman who once faked a concussion to steal a dog.”

“That was strategic,” Yelena sniffed. “And for the dog.”

Bucky leaned in, blue eyes bright despite the grizzled deadpan. “You owe me a beer,” he said. “Also, if you ever try to die on my watch again, I’m duct taping you to the couch.”

Clint raised his good hand. “Seconded. Also, for the record, I maintain my headshot is cleaner than yours. The camera angle on that street was unflattering.”

Rio’s eyebrows tried to climb; the tape kept them in place. “Clint.”

“Fine,” he sighed, and his mouth softened. “You saved our girl.” He nodded toward Agatha. “Thank you.”

Rio turned her head, only a few degrees, careful of everything that hurt, and met Agatha’s eyes. Whatever passed between them was quiet and sure and enormous. Agatha brushed a stray curl back, eyes glistening again.

Alice set the flowers on the rolling tray with reverence and stepped close.” I’ll brief you when you’re ready,” she murmured, ever the analyst, even with tears in her lashes. Then, softer: “Don’t ever scare me like that again, boss.”

“Wasn’t… in the plan,” Rio rasped.

“Yeah, well,” Alice said, voice wobbling. “Update your plan.”

Lilia edged closer, fingers twisting the stem of a paper coffee cup. “Hello, darling,” she said to Rio, voice warm and aching. “You don’t have to talk. I just needed to see you breathing.” She bent and pressed a kiss to Rio’s hair. “Thank you for coming back.”

Wanda stepped up to Agatha’s shoulder and squeezed it, then leaned down so Rio could see her. “You stupid, brave woman,” she said softly. “Thank you.”

Rio wet her lips. “I promised,” she said. “I keep promises.”

Wanda’s gaze softened but held an iron edge. “Good. Then promise me you will live a very long time to keep making them.”

“Deal.”

The doctor came and went, pleased with the metrics, warning everyone that exhaustion would hit like a freight train in minutes. He wasn’t wrong. Ten smiles and five jokes later, Rio’s eyes started to drift. Agatha laughed when Rio tried to scowl about it.

“Sleep,” Agatha said, brushing her knuckles along Rio’s cheek. “We’re not going anywhere.”

Rio’s fingers tightened on hers. “Stay.”

“Always.”

Her eyes slid shut.

The room didn’t exhale, exactly. It settled, like a house in winter shifting closer around its people.

They kept the visit short on purpose. One by one, they kissed Rio’s hair or squeezed her hand, each leaving a little offering of levity on the tray: a packet of gummy bears (Yelena), a tiny knitted raven someone had delivered to the front desk (Bucky), a Polaroid of the team making faces with a “GET WELL, BOSS” scrawled by Clint. Wanda promised to check in and bring decent tea. Lilia promised soup. Jen promised to battle producers with the fury of a thousand volcanoes. Alice promised to hack the sun if necessary.

Maria and Natasha lingered. Maria touched Agatha’s shoulder. “We’ll run interference,” she murmured. “If you want the next hour alone…”

Agatha nodded, grateful. “Thank you.”

The door closed softly. The machines hummed. Rio slept, breathing a touch deeper than before. Agatha watched her, then glanced at the riot of offerings on the tray and exhaled a laugh that was half-sob.

She reached for her phone. For a long moment, she just held it, thumb hovering. She wasn’t sure what to say to the world. Then she thought of the hundreds of thousands of messages, gentle, furious, terrified, that had ballooned her inbox. She thought of the hateful speculation, too. But mostly she thought of the people who loved them, who needed to see what she was seeing: a stubborn chest rising and falling; a future unspooling again.

Agatha shifted onto the edge of the mattress, careful of wires, and angled the camera. She leaned in close to Rio’s sleeping face, pressed their joined hands into the frame, and snapped a soft, grainy selfie. There were tears in her lashes, a smear of hospital light on Rio’s cheek, and the faintest curve of a smile still clinging to Rio’s mouth like a promise.

She typed, then deleted, then typed again.

@Mother_Harkness: She’s awake. She’s here, and it’s time I say this out loud: Rio Vidal is the love of my life. 💜 She has saved me more times than anyone should ever have to. I wouldn’t be here without her. No rumor, no threat, no one will ever take what we have. Not from me. Not from her. Thank you, my love, for protecting me, for standing by me, and for teaching me that home is a person, not a place.

Always, always yours.

—Agatha

She hovered one heartbeat more. Then she hit Post.

The effect was immediate, her screen a fireworks show of hearts, comments, tears. Within a minute, she was trending again, but for once the wave felt warm, not ravenous.

Maria peeked back in with Natasha, saw the phone in Agatha’s hand, and arched a brow. Agatha blew out a breath and turned the screen around. Maria’s mouth softened into that rare, sun-warm smile Agatha had only seen her wear with Natasha and Rio.

“What did you do?” Natasha asked, amused and suspicious in equal measure.

“Committed to joy,” Agatha said. “And told the truth.”

“Bold,” Natasha murmured. “On brand.”

“Also,” Agatha added, a wicked spark back at last, “I promised the internet a group photo.”

Natasha groaned. “Of course you did.”

Agatha grinned and beckoned. “Come on, Red. We’ll be quiet.”

A minute later, the door reopened like a clown car. Yelena wedged in, hauling Bucky by the sleeve. Clint limped in with theatrical sighs. Alice practically teleported. Jen dragged a white coat over her pajamas to look “respectable for posterity.” Lilia straightened her blouse and dabbed at her eyes. Wanda slipped in with Vision and Tommy hovering just outside the threshold, solemn and protective.

“Okay,” Agatha whispered, lifting her phone. “Squeeze.”

“This is absurd,” Natasha said, already leaning in.

“Shut up and look intimidating,” Yelena ordered, then ruined it by beaming.

Agatha framed the shot so that Rio’s sleeping face was centered, everyone else a crescent of tenderness around her. Agatha leaned down and pressed their entwined hands to her heart, then clicked. The picture captured everything: bruises and laughter and mascara smudges and a dozen different kinds of love all aimed in the same direction.

She posted it as a carousel; the second slide was the quiet selfie from moments ago.

@Mother_Harkness
 Family, by blood and by battle. Thank you for loving us. 💜

And then she put the phone face down and took Rio’s hand again. “Sleep,” she whispered. “I’ll handle the internet.”

The internet, for once, behaved like a cathedral.

@Agathario_Updates: Agatha Harkness CONFIRMS relationship with Bodyguard Rio Vidal with intimate photo from Hospital bed, after months of speculation. Fans go Ferel #Agatharioisreal #AgathaHarkness #RioVidal #BodyguardxClient

@covenkiddo: SHE’S AWAKE. I’m crying in a Target. I don’t even care. #RioVidal #AgathaHarkness

@femmeranger: “the love of my life”????? THEY SAID IT WITH THEIR WHOLE CHESTS I’M ON THE FLOOR

@inkandiron: That group photo… the way they’re all orbiting her like planets around a sun. Found family is real, and it’s here.

@BelowDeckButMakeItGay: Rio sleeping like “I fought twenty mercs and a final boss, brb.” Agatha smiled like “my girlfriend’s a superhero.” I’m unwell. #Agatharioisreal

@theatrewitch93: To everyone who said it was a PR thing: sucks to suck!!!!! Love won!! (Also donate to veteran recovery orgs in Rio’s honor—linking a few below.) 💜

@softbutferal: Agatha posting from the ICU and calling Rio the love of her life… I have never known peace, and I never want to. #Agatharioisreal #RioVidal

@tattooed_bodyguardstan: RIO. VIDAL. END ME. (respectfully.) So happy she’s okay. Protect them both at all costs.

@StepOnMeAgathario: Not me tearing up at 7 am over a woman holding her girlfriend’s hand in a hospital bed. This is what the internet should be for.. #Agathario

@Agathas_Whore: The way that one photo said: grief, grit, gratitude. I hope the world treats them gently now.

By the time the nurse returned to adjust Rio’s fluids, the comment count had become a tide Agatha couldn’t even pretend to count. She didn’t try. She looked at Rio instead.

As if hearing her name called in a distant room, Rio stirred. Her lashes fluttered. She opened her eyes, unfocused, found Agatha’s face again, and smiled the tiniest bit more.

Agatha leaned close. “You just broke the internet,” she murmured.

Rio blinked, confused and amused. “Again?”

“Again,” Agatha said, and kissed the back of her hand. “Get some rest, darling. We’ve got a lot of living to do.”

From the doorway, Maria caught Natasha’s hand. Yelena tucked the blanket with a tenderness she’d deny. Bucky let out a breath like he’d been holding it his whole life. Wanda wiped at her eyes and smiled at Agatha over the bedrail.

The machines kept time. The room was warm. And for the first time in days, the future felt possible, threaded through with pain, yes, but bright with the ordinary, defiant miracle of waking up and choosing each other all over again.

 

The room was quiet in the way hospitals are quiet, soft machines humming, a murmur of footsteps in the hall, the slow, stubborn beep of the heart monitor pacing out proof of life. Rio lay propped up on pillows, bandages peeking from beneath the loose neck of the gown, the blue-tinged tape along her clavicle stark against her tan skin. An IV dripped steadily. The air smelled faintly of antiseptic and stale coffee. It's been a week since the ambush, and the Doctors still refuse to send Rio home. 

Agatha sat in the hard-backed chair at Rio’s bedside with her knees pulled up, one hand wrapped around Rio’s fingers like a lifeline. She’d been that way for an hour. Longer, maybe. Time ran funny in rooms like this.

“You should try to sleep,” Rio murmured, voice rough with healing and disuse.

Agatha huffed a laugh that didn’t reach her eyes. “You first.”

Rio’s mouth tilted. “I would, but there’s a very persistent actress stonewalling my attempts.”

“That actress has a few notes, actually,” Agatha said, but her smile faltered as she studied Rio’s face—the purpled bruises, the split lip, the tiredness behind the dark eyes she loved. The words arrived without warning, thick and unwieldy. “I keep replaying it,” she said softly. “The sound of the metal. The way you threw yourself over me. Watching you—” She swallowed; it hurt. “I can’t stop thinking that every person who touches me ends up hurt. Or worse.”

Rio’s fingers tightened as much as the tape and swelling would allow. “Hey.” She waited until Agatha looked up. “You don’t get to carry that. Not on my watch.”

Agatha shook her head, tears threatening. “How many times is this going to happen, Rio? How many bullets? How many scars? I love you, and I feel like loving me is a hazard sign.”

“It’s my job,” Rio said simply. “And even if it weren’t—” She made a careful breath, the edges of pain sharpening her inhale; she didn’t let it show beyond a blink. “Even if I’d met you as a stranger in a grocery store, I’d put myself between you and whatever came through the door. It’s who I am. It’s what I choose.”

Agatha stared at her, jaw tight, trying to hold the line. “And if you can’t choose it anymore?”

“Then I’ll choose you in a different way.” Rio’s voice softened, the husk in it more tenderness than strain. “I don’t regret a single scar, Agatha. Not one. Not the old ones, not the new ones.” Her eyes warmed; the tears there surprised even her. “You are the love of my life. I have never meant those words more. I would do it all again, a thousand times, and I would not lose a minute of sleep over the choice.”

The dam went. Agatha bowed her head and pressed their joined hands to her lips. “I hate that you were alone on that street,” she whispered. “I hate that I watched, and I couldn’t—”

“You were not alone,” Rio said. “You were with me. We were with each other. That’s the only part I care about.”

Agatha scooted closer, careful of the IV line, and lay her cheek against Rio’s forearm. Her voice, when it came, had steadied. “Say it again.”

“That you’re the love of my life?” Rio’s mouth curved. “Gladly. You are the love of my life, Agatha Harkness. You are my home.”

Agatha exhaled like she’d been underwater. “Okay,” she said, a small, tear-wet laugh. “Okay.”

A soft knock preceded the doctor and a nurse. Agatha sat back, wiping under her eyes, and threaded her fingers with Rio’s again like a dare to anyone to separate them.

“Ms. Vidal,” the doctor said gently, checking the monitors. “How’s the pain?”

“Manageable,” Rio lied on instinct.

The nurse snorted. “That’s Ranger for ‘eight out of ten.’”

Rio’s eyes smiled. “Seven.”

The doctor’s expression held that complicated mix of relief and restraint Agatha had grown to loathe, good news with barbs. “You’ve stabilized well. The lung puncture was the most concerning, but your oxygenation is improving. The shoulder wound was clean; we’re watching for nerve irritation. The abdominal trauma from the crash and the internal bleeding are… well, you know the list.”

“Greatest hits,” Rio said.

“Here’s what I need you both to hear,” the doctor continued, eyes shifting kindly to Agatha as if anticipating the storm there. “Recovery won’t be weeks. It will be months. You’ll need extensive physical therapy; respiratory work for the lungs, range-of-motion in the shoulder, rebuilding core strength, and endurance. You’ll be exhausted by how slow it feels. You’ll think you should be further along. That’s normal.”

Rio nodded. “Understood.”

The doctor paused. “I can’t promise you’ll be able to return to active, on-your-feet, high-threat security work at the same level. I can say we’re hopeful you’ll regain full function with time and adherence to rehab. But your body has been through a war.”

Agatha’s grip tightened; Rio squeezed back.

“Best case,” the doctor said, gentler now, “you come back strong. Different, maybe, but strong. Worst case, we pivot to a new normal that doesn’t ask your body to keep stepping in front of bullets.”

The words hung between the three of them, heavy and impossible.

“We’ll be scheduling consults with the PT team today,” the nurse added. “If you’ve got a place you can heal that’s quiet, low stress, with support, that’s ideal.”

Rio looked at Agatha. She didn’t need to say it. Agatha was already nodding. “We have a place,” she told the doctor. “We’ll make it work.”

When they left, the room felt smaller, the doorway too far. Agatha stood and leaned over, kissing Rio’s knuckles. “We’re going to your cabin,” she said. “No press, no paps, no gunshots, no beeping machines except maybe a coffee maker.”

Rio let out a breath that sounded dangerously like relief. “You okay with that? It’s… far.”

“Good,” Agatha said. “Let it be far. Wanda and Vision need far. Tommy needs far.” She swallowed around the ache. “We all need somewhere the world can’t find us for a minute.”

Rio nodded. “Wanda will say no.”

“She will,” Agatha agreed. “And then she’ll look at Tommy, and she’ll say yes.”

They let the quiet return, not empty this time, but planning-quiet. The kind where a future could be drawn, faint pencil lines waiting for ink.

“We’ll need to split the team,” Rio said after a moment, her voice turning practical even as it rasped. “Maria, Jen, and Lilia in L.A. to manage fallout. Sonya and Steve on the house, plus two of the Widows rotating with them. Alice can run Intel Remote from the SHIELD office and send us encrypted updates. Bucky and Natasha can come east, rotate with Yelena and Clint. Minimal footprint at the cabin, no convoys, nothing that draws eyes.”

Agatha nodded. “I am already done filming. What little we have left to shoot, I can just tell the studio to wait, that I need time to grieve and rest, they won't argue.” A shadow crossed her face, then steadied. 

“What about Hela and Evanora?” Rio asked, because she had to.

“We let them scream into the void,” Agatha said, surprising both of them.

Rio stared at her for a long moment, pride unfurling in her chest like a flag. “You’re brave.”

“I’m tired,” Agatha corrected. “If I can’t be safe, I can at least be stubborn.”

Rio laughed, then winced, then laughed again because the wince made Agatha fuss and she liked being fussed over by her. “We leave when the doctors clear me for transport,” she said. “We take a jet. No flight plans under our names. Thor’s contact can arrange it.”

Agatha’s eyes softened at “we.” “We,” she echoed.

A nurse popped in to adjust the drip and left them a minute later. Agatha sank back into the chair and traced a circle on the inside of Rio’s wrist where her pulse beat strong and steady. “You’re coming home,” she said. “And I don’t mean L.A.”

Rio’s lashes lowered. “I know.”

They talked through the small things next, the ones that make a life. Who would pack what? Which blankets from the cabin did Agatha want ready? Whether the old record player worked or if they were finally going to replace it. What movies they’d bring for Tommy that didn’t feature explosions or loss? It felt defiant to plan these things. Holy, even.

After a while, Rio’s eyes grew heavy. Agatha saw it, stood, and bent to kiss her forehead. “Sleep,” she whispered. “I’ll be here when you wake up.”

“You always are,” Rio murmured, drifting.

Agatha settled back into the chair, fingers threaded with hers, and stared out at the thin slice of city visible through the blinds. Somewhere out there, the Seven, what remained of them, were hungry, circling. Somewhere out there, headlines were sharpening their teeth. Somewhere, grief lay like fog over a home that had once been loud with laughter.

But here, in this narrow, humming room, was a different truth. A stubborn, ordinary miracle. Breath in, breath out. A heartbeat steadying. A promise made and remade.

Agatha leaned forward and pressed their joined hands to her chest. “We’ll go to the woods,” she whispered, for both of them. “We’ll heal. We’ll come back when we choose, not because they force us to.”

Rio’s fingers twitched in her sleep, as if answering. Agatha smiled, the first one that didn’t fracture at the edges in days, and let her head tip back against the wall. She could almost smell pine and lake water, feel the sun on the dock, hear Rio’s laugh as if it were already summer again.

They would go. They would rest. And when they returned, it would not be as wounded animals limping from a fight, but as women who had remembered what they were fighting for.
